“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper,
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary journal.
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System-one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance,
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is nob
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
?r.d has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim thab every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established A us-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical, it points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies. •
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushraan of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Madriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

SATURDAY, JULY 30, 1892.
“I, Dibbs, am a Republican.”
It is unwise to trust any politician until he
is dead, and unless he has been dead a very
long time it is unwise to trust him even then.
He who puts his faitli in a live politician is
almost always “ left,” and he who puts his
faith in anybody else is generally “left”
also. Premier Dibbs, of New South Wales,
is the latest evidence of the truth of this
axiom A few days ago he was a unique
figure in Australian politics. , lie was an
honest man, inasmuch as no one had ever
found him using his high position for any
underhand private purpose, and every man
is honest until he is found out—also a good
many are honest even after they are found
out. He had never run a bogus bank or a
swindling insurance-company, nor had he
obtained an overdraft by any means which
wouldn’t bear inspection, nor cut up any
estate into small allotments and sold them
under shady circumstances. He was the
first avowedly republican -Premier who ever
held office in Australia, and ho looked like
the one republican who would be true to his
faith unto the end, and never sell himself for
a handful of silver or a scrap of ribbon to
put in his coat. Such shoddy creatures as
Robert Stout might preach democracy for
years and then grab at the first cheap title
that came along, like a dog snatching at a
raw chop, but the general impression was
that Dibbs had a genuine contempt for the
little, mean, tinpot distinctions which Vic-
toria throws to “ loyal at three a
penny. But all this is past, and ano'.hor
idol is broken-down on the highway.
Dibbs is now only a K.C.M.G., aim g
with the King of Siam, and the dingy poten-
tate of Zanzibar, and a certain dirty native
dignitary at Dongola, and a mysterious yellow
man in Eastern Asia whose full name is too
long for publication ; and he has only escaped
being a brother-dignitary to the King of the
Cannibal Islands through the timely decease
of that fish-smelling potentate who lately
went to sleep with his fathers at Honolulu,
and thus left a vacancy in the order of St.
Michael and St. George.
It is probably safe to assume that Dibits is
a republican no longer. Whether he over
was one, or whether he over imagined he was
one arc matters which only the new
K.C. M.G. can decide. Possibly he doesn’t
know himself. But the essence of republi-
canism is equality f and the man who pines
after, or accepts under whatever pressure,
decorations granted by Queen Victoria,
is . virtually pledged to prop up the im-
perial connection —partly out of gratitude
for the title lie has already got, and partly
for the sake of those which ho may get in
the future. The process by which Hires
sold himself was the same by which almost
every prominent Australian who visits Eng-
land is sold sooner or later. He was bought
with flattery and champagne, and the visions
of bare-backed females in fashionable draw-
ing-rooms, and the glare of gas-lights, and
the dance at aristocrat iQ. balls, and by largo,
greasy dinners, and conversations with
royalty, and other matters of the same des-
cription. And as the end of all these things
lie has received, as his price, an alleged
honour which sits upon him as gracefully as
a tall hat oh a naked-ttbertginai. '•
• • •
4 So far as we are aware no Australian has
ever yet gone to England and returned with-
out a title except the one who couldn’t get a
title, or who was offered one which he con-
sidered too humble for acceptance. Also, so
far as our knowledge goes, no Australian has
ever yet failed to go to England in search of
a title, save the one who couldn’t raise the
funds for the journey by overdraft at a
bogus bank or otherwise, or who had no
hope of getting anything for his pains,
or who had his title sent out to him and
therefore didn’t require to go. The re-
publican party consists of the great mass
which has no earthly hope of ever becoming
a K.C.M.G., just as the Land Nationalisation
party consists of the huge majority which
hasn't even a quarter of an acre o£ land to
nationalise, and the preachers of religious
equality always represent those churches
which arc too small and feeble to have any
chance to get on top. A nd, therefore, the
hope of republicanism lies chiefly in the
fact that no monarch is likely to raise the
whole of the Australian population to the
Order of St. Michael and St. George, and
if he did so the “ distinction ” would lose its
value, and all the K.C.M.G.s would pre-
sently become republicans for .want of any-
body underneath to envy them. And, at
the same time, the groaQfcar of republican-
ism lies in the fact that the; party never has
a reliable leader, for-whenever a leader
grows sufficiently strong to be worth buying, ho
is shoved along to market by his womankind
and purchased with a remnant of ribbon for
his. chest, and a splash of ink to put in front
of his ignoble cognomen. Up to date the
same poor, mean little “ honour ” has bought
them all--save and except only poor old Joe
Bosisto, of eucalyptus oil fame, with his
name blown in the bottle as a guarantee
against fraudulent imitations, who became
only a small, cheap C.M.G., and then
vanished out of public life, and took his three
commonplace letters with him into obscurity.
* * *
In all probability, however, Premier Dibbs
will be, for a time at least, a greater success
with his title than he was without it. The
dual-vote crowd will look more favourably
upon him than they used to do, and the
public-house interest—that is to say, the large
public-house which has-no mortgage upon it
—will be more friendly towards him, and
the “loyal” remnant which still survives in
the land will reverence him because of his
associations with royalty, and society, as
represented by the unclothed person at the
theatre, will view him in an altogether new
light, and Sydney Burdekin will either
reverence him because he has got a title or
will gnash his teeth upon him because he
didn’t get one himself—-no one can tell
which. Therefore, Dibbs will probably hold
office longer than he might otherwise have
donjb, and draw more salary, and be a bigger
feature at social functions where cheap sherry
and sandwiches predominate, and be alto-
gether a larger and more imposing mediocrity
than he otherwise would have been. But he
will never be anything but a large mediocrity
to the end of his days. He has fallen, amid
the secret laughter of the Colonial Office pimps,
into that dreary groove which leads to “loyal-
ism,” and much whooping about the National
Anthem, and much grovel about the “ be-
loved Queen.” There are many varieties of
social distinction,.but they all lead to con-
servatism and grovel in the end. The
woman who owns the green silk dress in a
bush township where no one else owns a
silk dress of any kind is always a conserva-
tive and an upholder of the established order
of things.' So is the woman who shakes
hands frequently with the mayoress, and the
back-blocks publican who was patronised
once by the Governor when he passed
through the district, and the tenor in the
choir, and the person who once saw the
Prince of Wales, and the individual who
owns a piano in a region where these instru-
ments a e scarce. Premier Dibbs, by virtue
of his new title, is now on a par with all
t hese, and the effect in his case will be the
same.
# # #
“The smallest distinctions thankfully re-
ceived ” is the notice that is hung on the
soul of almost every Australian politician,
and of almost every other politician, and of
almost everybody else, whether he is a poli-
tician or not. Even the New South Wales
Labour - party has become comparatively
tame since its members got admission
to Government House, and Softhat, the
Labour M.P., is hardly the firebrand
he used to he since he has risen to he
a member of Tatters all’s at a subscrip-
tion of three guineas per annum. Probably
Premier Dibbs was as good a republican as
the average, in which case it is a dreary out-
look for republicanism ; possibly he was a
little worse than the average, in which case
it is a solemn lookout for himself and his
own soul. 'lt may be that there were twelve
Iscariots am ong the apostles—there generally
are, in fact —-only the other eleven were left
in the rush for the cash, and, if so, the world
has wasted over eighteen centuries of useless
vituperation. But Dibbs’ apostacy is aggra-
vated by the fact that ho looked more
honest than the average more honest
than a great many honest men can
afford to look. The country was not
surprised when Dalley accepted a title,
for Dalley was in many respects a politi-
cal fossil, whose opinions were those of
the dark ages, and his one axiom was
that Australia should'be prepared to sacrifice
its last coin and its- last -man, if need be, for
the sake of the Gdelbhs and their relations.
Neither was it surprised at Griffith, for
Griffith was a transparent sham ; nor at
M‘ll wraith, who was a corpulent money-
grubber and a man who hungered after
greasy dinners and corner allotments, nor at
almost any other of the shoddy knights who
make themselves ridiculous in the land.
But Dibbs, the avowed republican, had
further to fall than any of them, and he slid
down with the ease of a man who was accus-
tomed to the work. As a republican he
desired to abolish royalty, and having
accepted what he evidently regards as a
favour from that institution, The Bulletin
presumes that in common gratitude he
doesn’t want to abolish it any longer. At
all events, it devoutly hopes that such is
the case, and that Dibbs is done with
republicanism. For if he still retains
his former professions he may, by some
misfortune, get a chance to go back on
them a second time for another title, and
he may do it at a time when his falling away
will be a more serious disaster. In accept-
ing his mean little decoration he has already
been an apostate to his old faith. If he again
advocates republicanism he will be an
apostate to his new faith, which is the afore-
said decoration. And if by so doing he gets
a chance, as he possibly may, to quit his
original creed once more for another gewgaw
he will have apostatised for the third time.
The man who gets a chance to apostatise
three times is a public calamity. For the
time being Dibbs has become an Imperialist,
and this paper devoutly trusts that he will
stay there.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A New Loan.
The New South Wales Government has floated
locally a new loan of £500,000, at par, the
interest being 44 per cent. The money is to be
expended on public works, the material for
which will be manufactured in the province—
except where contracts made by the Parkes
Ministry block the way —therefore the principal
■and interest will both remain in the com try.
The interest is just one half per cent above the
average rate paid for British capital in the old
Foreign-trade days, when New South Wales
had a cheap, imported Premier, and used to
import cheap British capital, and spend it in
buying imported steel rails and imported rolling-
stock, and the constantly accumulating load of
debt assisted no local industry, and furnished
no employment save to the men who got a tem-
porary job at shovelling dirt on the new
lines, and laying down the imported rails, and
securing them with imported bolts, under the
supervision of imported officials. The half-
million which has been borrowed in the pro-
vince will presently go back into circulation
along with the interest, and —if the New South
Wales Government must still continue its bor-
rowing policy—there is no earthly reason why
it should not be borrowed and expended on
public works over and over again without any
end or visible limit. So long as there is
£500,000 of available cash in N.S. \V.,and so long
as the Government remains true to its Protec-
tionist principles by spending its funds within the
province only, there is absolutely no reason why
the State shouldn’t be able to borrow a hundred
millions if it wants tooutof that onelittle amount.
The theory that the Australian Governments
must raise money in England because Australia
has not the money to lend is utterly illogical.
If there is even £lOO,OOO available in the country,
and the State borrows it, and expends it locally,
and pays the interest to local bondholders, it
can keep on borrowing the principal and the
interest over again till it owes as much as the
most reckless financier can possibly desire. It
is only the hungry longing of the Foreign-trade
party to send the money out of the country in
the purchase of foreign material, which makes
it necessary to bring in foreign capital to keep
the nation going, and then the constant neces-
sity for sending out more money in payment of
interest to foreign bondholders makes it neces-
sary to bring in more foreign capital, and so on
ad infinitum.
♦-
More Freedom of Contract.
The other day Judge Worthington, of Vic-
toria, sentenced one William Smith to two
years’ imprisonment with hard labour for steal-
ing a ring, and while passing this sentence —
which, fora first offence, was a disgrace to any
civilised land—he took the opportunity to insult
the nation at large with some utterly gratuitous
and irrelevant remarks concerning the prin-
ciple of freedom of contract. Smith’s case
was a very ordinary one. He was out of work,
hungry, penniless, and hopeless—in other
words, he was one more victim of that commer-
cial depression which was brought about by th e
respectable swindlers who have lately been
floating banks and creating land-booms in
Victoria, - and who dine and liquor-up
with Judges’' in between times. The weather
was cold, and the homeless Smith seems to have
come to the conclusion that gaol was the only
refuge open to him. Therefore he deliberately
broke a jeweller’s window in open daylight,
and took out a ring, am] was promptly arrested.
Apparently he didn’t want the ring and only
desired to be locked up, but Judge Worthing,
ton chose to regard him as a hardened offender
and seemed to consider the jact that he was
hungry and unemployed as an aggravation of
his offence—in fact, to all appearance, he got
six months for theft, and the other eighteen for
being out of work. “ Men have only themselves
to blame,” Said the majesty of the law, “ ii
they cannot find employment, inasmuch as they
persistently refuse reasonable wages which they
recklessly fix on a scale above that which
employers are able to afford.” Now, there was
not one atom of evidence before the court to
show that William Smith had “ recklessly
fixed ” his wages on any scale which employers
couldn’t afford, or that he had refused any
wages whether reasonable or unreasonable, or
that he wasn’t even willing to work for his
board and the privilege of sleeping on top of
the coals, with no wages whatsoever. The
Judge’s harangue was only brutal, unreasoning
class bigotry ; he had heard that some men with
tattered clothes and broken boots wouldn’t
accept reasonable wages, and. as Smith’s
clothes were tattered he held that as sufficient
evidence that he belonged to them. Moreover,
Mr. Worthington’s own wages are fixed at a
higher rate than his employers —the nation—are
able to afford, and he persistently refuses
reasonable pay, which, in his case, and
considering his intellectual calibre, would
be about £2 per week. He has never had
to get up at six o’clock on a cold, wintry morn-
ing, and wait in the rain for two hours or so
outside the door of somebody who has advertised
for a porter, and fight with a hundred others
for a front place, and then find that the billet is
filled up, and have to go away for another day
of starvation and wretchedness. It is a pity
indeed that such Judges as Mr, Worthington
can’t be worked on the same great, free and en-
lightened principle that they are so fond of. It
would be a cheerful thing if the Government,
instead of appointing these individuals for life
at a big salary, Mould simply advertise every
morning for a Judge to sentence drunks and
petty thieves by contract, and it would
be a gladsome spectacle to see Worth-
ington struggling with fifty other Judges
outside the Chief Secretary’s door in the
hope of getting the job, and then going away
disconsolate because some bigger Judge trampled
over him, and got there first, and agreed
to sentence the whole lot for 3s, (id., before
he could get in. The entire Australian Bench
seems to suffer from a conviction that it is paid
to be a standing advocate of cheap labour and
non-unionism, and considering how frightfully
overpaid the Bench is, and that its members
belong to the most grasping and arrogant
trades-union that ever existed, this conduct is
either hypocrisy or utter heartlessness—or
both.
—
Mr. G. H. Reid, alleged leader of the New
South Wales Opposition, tries to trap the
Single-tax vote by the assertion that “Free-
trade would compel future Governments, if
increased taxation was required, to get it by
means of a tax on the unimproved value of land,
instead of out of the pockets of the masses.” It
is utterly impossible to suppose that Reid
believes this glaring rubbish—but he probably
thinks that a Freetrade audience is weak
enough to swallow anything. New South Wales
has had nearly fifty years of Freetrade already,
and though it lias had some very hard times its
fiscal policy (no matter how badly a stiff land-tax
was required) has never yet “ compelled ” it to
resort to a land-tax—it has only forced it
to collect over £2,000,000 per annum in
, Customs duties and to make up its short-
age out of loans. England has also had
nearly fifty years of Freetrade, and Free-
trade has never “compelled” it to impose a
land-tax either—it has only compelled it to
levy a.,larger .amount in Customs duties than
had ever before been dreamt of by the most
protected country upon earth. Freetrade, in
fact, has never yet compelled any country to
resort to a land-tax, though it has driven them
to Customs duties, income-tax, wholesale bor-
rowing, and all manner of expedients, and there
is no reason to believe it will produce any
belter results in the future than it did in the
past. And, at the best, Mr. Reid’s assertion
only means that Freetrade will make the country
so poor and wretched and utterly bankrupt
that it will have to levy upon the big landlords,
which merely signifies that Freetrade is a bad
thing which may by chance lead to a good
result. And in the same way if Mr. Reid died
of over-eating it might lead a whole lot of
gluttonous people to dine more moderately for
fear of a similar fate, but it doesn’t necessarily
follow that it would be a good thing for Mr.
Reid to die of over-eating. And, by exactly
the same process of reasoning, if Parkes fell
over a precipice with fatal results it might lead
to—but, on second thoughts, we won’t follow
this aspect of the case any further.
— AS;
The Age suffers from another of its periodical
attacks of music-fever. The old fiction that
Melbourne is ever sighing for a permanent
orchestra of 50 performers and hungering for
the lofty melodics of great masters is set up m
type once again. Thin, pallid reasons for the
failure of orchestral concerts up to date are
followed by attenuated arguments to prove
that other concerts will certainly pay if the
“ music-loving public ” are appealed to. Bet,
all the while, the practical caterers for amuse-
ment, who arc open to make money by supply-
ing anything in which money is, have
reckoned up the musical public as a
“ bad mark.” It consists mainly of professors
and their little cliques of pupils and admirers.
The professors worship Beethoven and Go.
“on the nod,” whilst the pupils and admireis
will come in armies to any concert if the sinews
of war be provided in the form of complimentary


i ckets- When a theatrical manager sees a long
recession of women in red opera-cloaks, dotted
re and there by a man in a full dress-shirt
Cont and a superior smile, marching down
cwaiiston-street, the manager knows that these
’ .lightened people are about to enjoy a mild
Cl usical entertainment gratis, or next door to
I” Perhaps each individual represents four-
' enn’orth of annual subscription to a more or
f. s amateur harmonic society. As items of
■ash payment, the musical worldlings seldom
‘ rabovea shilling, and they ‘ ‘ blow ” a good deal
s / oU t the support they are contributing when
q ie humble coin aforesaid is dashed down
regardless of expense. J. C. Williamson
■ecently appealed to the musical world with
first-class bills-of-fare at a moderate tariff, but
a inad bull might have wandered around the
Princess’s for a week without finding a red
opera-cloak to butt at. The Age affects to
believe that the people who didn’t come were
fearful of entering a theatre, and would have
rolled up to the same feast of sounds if given in
more serious class of building. They were
fearful of paying fair prices, and their absence
was entirely owing to the scarcity of “ paper.”

The other day a S.M. Herald scribe w’as told
off to write up the “ Produce Trade of Sussex -
street, Sydney,” for his paper. Says he, inter
alia in deploring the almost total absence of
the' home-grown potato from the Sydney
market:
Even the new tariff upon which the Protectionists
oased such sanguine expectations has made no difference
m this respect.
Of course it never occurred to this poor creature
that potatoes are not grown all the year round,
but are planted during August and September ;
; md seeing that the present New South Wales
tariff did not come into operation until
December last its benefits could not be secured
before the 1892 harvest. But this typical
Heraldite was no doubt instructed to import
into his article a hunk of Foreign-trade argu-
ment and he did it. Emboldened by his success
in “Freetrade ” logic, lie goes on to say :
Many agents say that the new duties have made no
iifferen.ee whatever in our home production. Some say
that they have led to an increased importation of seed
(potatoes).
A complete argument in favour of the duties.
For is not the seed intended for next month’s
planting, and will not the duties do exactly
what Protection, if sufficiently drastic,
always does —secure New South Wales’ own
market for her own people ? There were
many other crass inaccuracies in the article
referred to, but this is a fair sample of
the mush which the Herald ladles out to its
readers in support of its defunct policy.
The Age, which so barefacedly threw cold
water upon the Premier Building Association
prosecution in its first stages, and “ bore up ”
for Mat. Larkin when that bloated embezzler
was first arrested, is now conspicuous by its
backwardness in coming forward to speak plain
truths about Munro and Davies. According
to Age cablegrams, Matthew’s scheme for
reconstructing the Mercantile Bank was
“ favourably received ” in London which
would now appear to be a cable cram
of the first water. The same influential rag
mildly observes that “ that unfortunate institu-
tion, the Real Estate Bank,” has got into a
“ hopeless muddle ”—a sneaking insinuation
that nobody is seriously to blame in the matter.
For the past two years, or nearly, the Age has
been unfortunate enough to turn frequently its
blind eye on big financial scandals, whilst the
Argus— the solemn, respectable Argus —has
led the Melbourne cry for investigation and
vengeance. The situation is full of melancholy
humour. One paper occasionally attacks poli-
tical abuses, and frequently overlooks, if it does
not actually attempt to cover up, grave private
scandals—the other follows exactly opposite
lines of proceeding. The Argus has un-
doubtedly scored heavily against its rival by
being spared any political or personal tempta-
tion to support financial “ wrong ’tins.” It has
often struck a sympathetic chord in the pockets
of the public who have dropped their savings,
and the most Radical shareholder or depositor
buys the Argus these times if he wants a little
consolation in his misery.
Dear Bulletin, —In your issue of 2nd July
you mention Sir Thomas MTlwraith having
“swept the country with his short-lived
Nationalist policy.” That policy was a bogus
one and would never have been accepted but
for the fact that the country was terrified by
Griffith’s display of Imperialism as shown by
bis Naval Defence Bill, and in their sudden
terror the people grasped at MTlwraith’s
sham Nationalist policy as drowning men are
said to grasp at straws. The present writer
was both pleased and grieved to see the people
catching on to the fraud—grieved that they
should be so easily imposed upon by a wily
trickster whose every action had been a direct
contradiction to what he was then preaching, and
pleased because the very fact of the people
catching on so eagerly showed that they were
(e sirous of a really national programme and
were quite prepared to go the entire. animal in
iat direction. MTlwraith saw the opportunity
_ * ie embraced it. Since then the Democratic
patty has increased wonderfully, and when the 1
general election comes next year there will be a 1
, r a ‘° re glorious opportunity than the elections

The awkward rebuke delivered to the S.M.
Herald by its own evening Echo for an in-
delicacy has occasioned much diversion. The
other morning, the Herald announced in
its cable messages, relative to the great fire
at St. John’s, N. 8., that the bishop of the
diocese fled from his pilace in his shirt. Now,
the function allotted to the Echo is judged by
readers of the two journals to be, in a general
way, to give public expression to all the nasty,
mean little things which the people who run
both journals want to say, but don’t care to
print in the Herald ; but it looks as if the
Echo man occasionally disrelishes the humilia-
tions put upon him, and seized what he thought
was a chance to get level with the bosses of
the Herald. Anyhow, when he reprinted that
telegram, he omitted the dreadfully improper
part of it. He was not going to publish
anything calculated to expose the clergv
to ridicule, or to “bring a blush to the
cheek of the young person ” (Podsnax'). The
Herald staff might I’evel in such ribaldry, but
the Echo —never ! So he printed the cablegi’am
in a form as abbreviated as the Herald sug-
gested that the bishop’s attire was. But just
here the clumsiness came in. People who had
read in the Herald that the bishop had bolted
from the flames “in his shirt and without
shoes,” and who subsequently read the Echo
with its significant omission, could only form
the conclusion that the latter was in receipt of
later cablegrams which didn’t even mention a
shirt. A bishop without gaiters is a spectacle
apt to shake one’s faith in an over-ruling Pro-
vidence, but a bishop darting from his palace
without so much as a shirt! —why it is calcu-
lated to turn a whole population to infidelity
and agnosticism.

In the Victorian Assembly, the other day, Mr.
Melville made some strong references to “ the
case of the Real Estate Bank, in which half the
transactions are alleged to have been a swindle,
and were mixed up in the public press with the
name of the gentleman who had gone to
England to represent the Government.” Sub-
sequently this attack was carried a little further
by Mr. J. A. Wallace, who caused a multitude
of councillors to sit up and emit a horrified
“Oh!” when he added that “people were
starving now through being led into financial
companies by Mr. Munro.” The way in which
these statements were met and fenced with by
Messrs. Balfour, J. M. Davies, Macßain,
and Sargood, opens up a great question. One
and all of that virtuous quartette protested
against the casting of any slur upon the char-
acter of a person who couldn’t explain every-
thing to everybody’s satisfaction because he
had gone to Hingland. The solemn impudence
of such a defence was sufficiently appalling
without the incidental remark from Sargood
that “the statements published in the papers
were to be regretted.” :
One of the main objections to an income
tax lies in the fact that it falls heaviest on the
“ straight ” man whose income is derived from
sources which cannot be concealed, while the
crafty speculator in stores, agencies, horses ct
similia, grins barefacedly when the collector sighs
over his schedule.
Dear Bulletin, —There were some whole-
souled fellows on the Warrego (Q.) not so very
many years ago. Blank was clerk to the Targoo-
lahmine Divisional Board, and his books were
things of beauty to all save Government auditors.
Fortunately, before one of these officials could
swoop down, it was discovered that Blank’s
accounts were not correct, and there was'a deficit
of .£4O. Things looked like a trip to St. Helena.
But Blank was a decent sort of a chap, the best
pard on the river in a four-handed game, and
members of the board weren’t going to stand quiet
and see him lagged. Blank must resign. At the
Board-meeting, when Blank’s resignation was
handed in, the newspaper man arose and moved
“ that Mr. Blank’s resignation be accepted witli
regret, and that an honorarium of £5O be presented
to him, in recognition of bis long and valued ser-
vice.” Carried, non. con. The deficit was made
up therefrom, and tiie odd tenner “blewed, ’ in
style, the same night, at the pub. most adjacent
to the courthouse. — The Breaker.
The GUILELESS ABSENTEE
Friends, never speak a word of ill
Of anyone who’s cleared—
Don't c-.1l him names, but deem him slill
A thing to be revered ;
Although his banks have come to grief,
An 1 you for assets sigh,
L°* nofhlnjr sbak" your fond belief
In human purity.
0 ! never say that facts are facts
Concerning I. or J.,
Who didn’t justify their acts
Before they skipped away ;
01 ne’er assume that shady games
And dealings aught but white
Can be alleged against the names
Of parties out of sight.
Think not of making Jim explain
Where all the money went;
Let Matthew D. at “home" remain,
Secure and innocent;
For whilst they dwell beyond their seas
And have their praises sung
By kindred souls and C.M.G.’s—
Why, you must hold your tongue.
All Governments it e’er behoves
Religiously to strive
To bolster up the class of coves
Called “ representative,”
And when they’ve served their Gracious Quce;i,
’Tis meet that you should know
Their characters are sweet and clean
And like the driven snow.
Friends, never breathe a thought of ill
Against the absentee ;
The boy who slopes with master’s till
Is guiltless, donchersee;
No more shall smart absconding clerks
Have warrants on their track,
For magistrates won’t hear remarks
Behind a fellow’s back.
A thousand men in Melbourne town
Are ruined, more or less,
And “calls” have come (of half-a-crown)
To widows in distress ;
And people fancy this and that—
But O ! they musn’t say
These nasty things of Jim and Mat,
For why ? They've gone away !
The Old Whim Horse.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
lie’s an old grey horse, with his head bowed sadly,
And with dim old eyes and a queer roll aft.
With the off-fore sprung and the hind screwed
badly,
And lie’s marked all round with the brands of
graft;
And the grey old horse comes to wait and wonder
Why by night and day now the whim is still,
Why no voice is heard, and the stampers’ thunder
Sounds forth no more from the shattered mill.
In that whim he worked when the night winds
wrestled
With the waving trees on the hill o’erheatl,
And by day when the “ keets ” in the blossom
nestled,
As the seasons flourished, and waned, and fled ;
And he knew his shift, and the whistle’s warning,
And he knew the calls of the boys below.
Through the years, at night, or at eve, or
morning,
He had taken his turn in the old whim bow.
Now the whim stands still, and the wheeling
swallow
In the silent shaft hangs her home of clay,
And the lizards flirt and the swift snakes follow
O’er the grass-grown brace in the summer day ;
And the corn springs high in the cracks and
corners
Of the forge, and down where the timber lies;
And the crows are perched like a band of
mourners
On the broken huts on the Hermit’s Rise.
All the hands have gone, for the rich reef paid
out,
And the company waits till the calls come in ;
But the old grey horse, like the claim, is played
out;
And no market’s near for his bones and skin,
So they let him live, and they left him grazing
On the hills, and oft in the evening dim
I have seen him stand on the rises, gazing
At the ruined brace and the rotting whim.
The floods rush high in the gully under,
And the lightnings lash at the shrinking trees,
Or the cattle down from the ranges blunder
As the fires drive by on the summer breeze,
Still the old grey horse at the right hour wanders
To the lonely ring, tho’ the whistle’s dumb,
And with head bowed low by the bow he ponders
Where the whim boy’s gone—why the shifts
don’t come.
But there comes a night when he sees lights
glowing
In the roofless huts and the ravaged mill,
When he hears again all the stampers going—
Though the huts are dark and the stampers
still—
When he sees the steam to the black roof clinging,
And its shadows roll on the silver sands ;
And he knows the voice of his driver singing,
And the knocker’s clang where the braceman
stands.
See the old grey horse, like a creature dreaming.
On the ring once more take his ’customed place;
But the moon’s bright beams on the ruins stream-
ing
Show the scattered timbers and the grass-grown
brace.
Yet he hears the sled in the smithy falling,
And the empty truck as it rattles back,
And the boy who stands by the anvil, calling ;
And he “turns,” and backs, and he “takes up
slack.”
While the old drum creaks, and the shadows
shiver,
As the wind sweeps by, and the hut doors close,
And the bats dip down in the shaft, or quiver
In the ghostly light—round the grey horse goes;
And he feels the strain on his untouched shoulder.
Hears the voice again that was dear to him,
Sees the forms he knew, and his heart grows
bolder,
As he works his shift in the ghostly whim.
He hears in the sluices the water rushing
As the buckets drain and the doors fall back.
When the early dawn in the east is blushing,
He is limping still round the old, old track.
Now he sways and sinks, but, the foe defying,
High his head he lifts, and his old eyes glow;
Then he pitches down in the circle, dying,
From the ring no more will the grey horse go.
In a gully green, where a dam lies gleaming,
And the bush creeps back on the worked-out
claims,
And the sleepy crows in the sun sit, dreaming,
On the timbers grey and the charred hut
frames,
Where the logs slant down, and the hare is
squatting
In the high rank grass by the dried up course,
Nigh a shattered drum and a king-post rotting,
Are the bleaching bones of the old grey horse.
Melbourne.
EDWARD DYSON.
The Daily News, Melbourne, commences,
like the Age, with a co-operative co.
A correspondent informs us that Sir Sam
Griffith’s well-known old horse is now a pensioner
at the Darling Downs Station of Mr. Gannon,
M.L.A., who bought him at Brisbane saleyards a
good while ago for £G or £7.
A Melbourne M.P. went to Gippsland, and
saw a sickly-looking city clerk, turned selector',
whittling with an axe at a forest giant, 350 ft.
high, his place being full of them. “ When do
you expect to be cleared, mate ?” “ Gawd knows.’'
English advt. :
TYTANTED, groom-gardener, one who could play
’’ the harmonium preferred; light place, hut
some experience desired. Salary, £l7, with lodging
(not board). —“Vicar,” Chilworth, Romsey.
In Adelaide R.C. Cathedral an Irish priest
was describing from the pulpit, in inimitable
brogue, the entry of the Saviour into Jerusalem
or somewhere. When he made the crowd of
Jews turn round impatiently upon the afflicted
beggar on the roadside and tell him, in the very
best Tipperary, “ Howld yer whist! ” evqn his
compatriots had to laugh.
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent,
“ Black Art,” does not tell the whole story of the
blind wood-cutter of Lefroy. The man’s name is
Lee. He not only lost his eyesight by the gun-
accident, but parted with a few fingers at the
same time. Lee has since been lamed for life by
a nasty chop on the knee, but he still goes out with
his horse and dray, selects his tree, falls it, cuts it
up into the required lengths, loads his cart, and
puts down ITis pile wherever it is required. Lee
has ceased to be a curiosity/in Lefroy, where the
fame of a man who can drink 27 pints of beer at a
standing is still as fresh as it was five years ago.—
Yours. Snell.

816 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, July 27,1892.
Mr Dear Moorabinda,—
The farewell benefit tendered to Mrs. Vanderveer
Greene, on 23rd, in Centennial Hall, was a success cf
numbers, and here and there of art, hut the latter only in
spots. For the singer whose simple song strikes jour
tympanum like the kick of a horse was pre-eminent in
shriek once or twice, and a decollette soprano actually
jumping into the air after a saw-sharpening top C, spoilt
part of the programme, which, I remember, was made
hideous by a villainous “likeness” of the charming
henefieiarj' that turned the lovelj- lady into something
like a hungry hawk.
But after a pill the preserve. Mrs. Van. Greene—in an
umbrella-skirted eau-de-nil silk gown—sang two songs,
multiplied by encores into four, with the breadth and
sweetness which are always hers, and was florallv com-
plime'ited with a basket of flowers and a very cleverly-
arranged floral banner of her nation. Mons. Deslouis
refused the encore for his dramatic rendering of
“ L’Africaise ; ” “ Willie ” Walsho made quite a scena of
“ Les Huguenots ; ” Poussard was there with his magnetic
fiddle ; likewise Kowalski furioso in an arrangement from
“ Don Giovanni ” (he never keeps to what he’s billed for);
these, with Charles Huenerhein (who had a hand in
everything) and ’Cello Strauss were responsible for those
portions of the programme which kept you from wonder-
ing what time it was.
The audience didn’t lack social axes at Mrs. Greene’s
benefit. In the Eastern balcony Lord and Lady Jersey
lent aV.R. glamour to the galleries, and downstairs we
had Mayor and Maj’oress Manning in the van. There
were many dresses—everybody had on one, of course, blit
the only distinctly swell frock in my eye-circle was a
cahhage-green silk—the very latest shade—which stood
out wonderfully in the Northern gallery. Lady Jersey
enlivened an old, sartorial friend with a chilly red opera-
cloak and committee flowers ; her eldest daughter appar-
ently wore pale-blue, hut the Centennial Hall is such a
desert that, unless jou take your telescope, you
can only vouch for what your immediate neighbour
is suffering in.
There was a meeting held last week in Sydney Town
Hall to talk about the collection of Australian women’s
work which is being got ready for Chicago exhibition.
Lady Jersey presided, with many sensible suggestions,
and it was definitely decided to hold, at the Town Hall,
during October, a display of all work sent in, prior to
shipping off to the land of wooden hams.
Through the laissez-faire nature of the Australian
woman, Lady Jersej r 's allies are becoming so desperate
for exhibits, that they will take meritorious work from
any woman, no matter if she has just arrived here for the
first time on the mail-steamer from " ’Ome.” For in-
stance, a very distinguished member of the works com-
mission (I won’t give names) went to a most hopelessly
English lady artist, fresh caught from Europe, and begged
her to let them have a painting which adorned her studio,
and which she had painted at Kensington Art School, and
brought out with her ! In vain she politely pleaded that
neither she nor her painting was Australian, but only a
point-blank “No!” got rid of the importunate seekers
after “Australian” women’s work! However, no harm
Is done by such little dodges, as one always approaches
an exhibition, or at least that part of it where women’s
and children’s efforts are set forth, with one’s tongue in
one’s cheek.
Don’t you think it rather a funny idea when you’re
giving an entertainment (with lots of patronage about it)
to have a list of almost every name in the audience
printed in full on the hind part of the programme? Not
only a boastful catalogue of Sir Thingummy and Lady
Asterisk, Sir So-and-So, the Boodler, and so on (the sort
of thing one always has to put up with), hut the labels of
almost everybody in the reserved area. They fixed it so
at a recent chavoo in Sydney—gave tho visiting-card
appellation of nearly every human being who had hooked
a scat—they even flourished the names of some of
the critics—of the evening-dress and toppy-hat genus, of
course! But the worst of it is that an idea like this
catches on at once, and I shan’t be a bit surprised if the
announcement—“ Your names on the back of the pro-
gramme on the reservation of two or more scats” becomes
quite a recognised bait for the half-crowns of the
populace.
The sixth performance of “Gondoliers,” at Her
Majesty’s, drew an audience which made a Melbourne
pressman draw a long breath and exclaim piteously, “I
haven't seen a house like this for six months!” And
another interesting thing was tho presence in front of
a certain pretty little actress’s flaneb, whom she marries
very shortly, and her consequent dodging of Ryley’s more
than usually Gondolie-y hugs. But what must it feel like
to sit and watch the idol of your dreams tickling some-
body else under the chin! By the way, can a certain
hounding actor have thrown out a splint, as the grooms
say, or what caused that unnatural undulation on one of
his ankles the other evening? The way his slim under-
standings have filled out since he came to Australia has
been matter for common enraptured comment.
Jennie Leo had a beg, good-tempered audience at her
benefit on 21st inst. at (he Imperial Opera House, hut
whoever arranged the programme spoilt the artistic
success of the affair by allowing a willowy lady amateur
of the inumhle-gumhlo stamp to goad the pit to madness
in the “ Happy Pair.” Hhe was personally a cheap litho-
graph copy of Brown-Potter, tousled hair, draggly cloth-
ing and all, and her "acting” wasn’t a bad rechauffe of
the same ladj’s lighter methods, hut the stage is not her
home all the same. Nature has dowered her with a voice
like the voice of the sluggard complaining from beneath
four feather-pillows, and unless she means to play star
rdles in a band-box she had better retire and reflect.
The indigenous Australienne, even when she has got
her waist down to nineteen inches and has learnt to stare
stonily, is not a highly-grained and polished human pro-
duct, and the mother of daughters must often look round
and wonder who there is to teach her Melpomene
“manners?” But there is nobody in the whole length of
this continent. When she leaves schoolrooms behind her
it rests with herself whether she will be a model of grace or
a galoot. She knows she shouldn’t yawn without a shelter,
and that she mustn’t sniff, but her governesses don’t go
much beyond that, and the average Australian girl is pro-
jected into sassiety with a visage absolutely untrained to
meet the many surprises of fashionable life. Don’t wonder
why I am jeremiadiiig like this—my inspiration is an
advt. in a London paper-an ordinary, every-day sort of
announcement which calls attention to the fact that Mrs.
Blank teaches “ grace of figure and expression ; how to
dress to look well; to walk ;to look young and beautiful ;
to smile ; to frown ; everything pertaining to the customs
and habits of gentlewomen !” Lovely, isn’t it ?as a revel-
ation what a charmingly natural creature the English
belle must be! Something like a mechanical toy with
directions to “press the button to the right and the doll
sings ‘ Annie Rooney.’ ” No wonder she finds her Aus-
tralian prototype “free and easy.” Imagine the uncouth-
ness of the girl who doesn’t know when it’s time to scowl
at you!
Woman’s manner reminds me that a health-journal tells
us to smile “with the eyes only,” because the amiable
creature who goes through life d la Cheshire cat is liable
to get as creased after a while as last year’s pippin. But
smiling with the eyes is the trick of the flirtatious, yet
discreet, female who wouldn’t let her victim presume for
worlds, but doesn’t mind letting him hope, you know.
Men don’t like the woman who makes eyes with an
unexpressive lower countenance ; they size her up as a
hypocrite smiling a mechanical smile for delusive pur-
poses. But, mind you, I’d rather have her than the other
sort who make monkey faces supposed to he captivating
whenever a pair of coat tails flickers in the east.
Who are the trio of female figures in tho centre
medallion on the Imperial Opera House act-drop? One
lady in dun colour is nursing her knees in mid-air with
arms like oars ; the middle Venus has apparently a green
life-buoy round her waist and simply endless limbs, and is
supporting a rose-pink female who hangs into space with
her arms coming out of her sides and a head like a bottle-
stopper.
A curious thing—at the eye-glass-managed haunt of
Thespis mentioned above —is to see how hard it is Un-
popular artists to play down to a pit and not up to a
gallery. There are no gods at the Imperial, and the lady
of tights and comic vocalism, when she directs a flashing
wink at where Olympia ought to he, either ogles some
patcr-familias who has come in with the “ girls ” because
somebody said there was a moral in it, or else her
coquetry wastes itself on the hole in (ho roof where the
lonely limelight man awaits ids opportunity. To remedy
this obvious defect how would it he if the management
suspended a few gods by the hair from the skylight until
the favourites get used to the theatre?
What I particularly remarked in the d.c. of a certain
theatre the other night was a recent-amateur-hut-now-
real-live actress, very red about the cheek-bones, of
course. The way tho stage-struck amateur, who has at
last “ got on,” puts on beauty in private must he a
caution to the old hands who arc mostly content lobe
seen by daylight the colour of a kidney potato.
Otherwise the circle was uninteresting, and one had
lots of time to consider Gourlay how he grew big enough
to fill that wonderful check suit in which he lets his hack
hair down and looks just like Daddy Hallewell in tartan
trews. Sirs. Bennett, an actress of the profile variety
who paints herself with a millet-broom, judging by the
effect, wandered through the farce in a couple of ordinary
garments and a ohippy-lookingdelaine cloak and fly-away
hat—just the sort of guise, though, in which to meet a
stray lover by moonlight in a public park. The only
other hit of fashion about the theatre was tiie treasurer’s
boutonniere, which seems to get larger with each new
play produced.
With women talking twenty to the dozen about all sorts
of subjects, one can’t exactly feel hurt at the conduct of
an old bachelor who resides in a Sydney suburb, and who,
to make uu for the lack of feminine society, has philo-
sophically provided himself with four magpies and three
cockatoos ! No wonder lie doesn’t get married.
Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society held their second
concert in the Centennial Hall last week, and renewed
their first success. The bill was classic and enjoyable, as
the encores showed, and (hough you can’t really goby
the shrieks of an audience—which is individually a
mother, or an uncle, or a sweetheart, or some sort of a
relative to the platform performers—your inner con-
sciousness tells you if the rapture is deserved or not.
And I felt glad at every repetition, for orchestral music
is scarce just now,
A pair of proud S.A. parents recently gave what must
have been a lively sort of entertainment it was a babies’
party to celebrate the birthday of their only child. 1 don’t
really know, mind you, that it was their only child, hut
philosophy teaches me that when there are eleven infants
tearing one another to pieces in the nursery, the fond
mamma doesn’t give eleven parties per annum.
At this infantile shivoo there were some sixty squallers
from six months to three years. One pauses to wonder
how the delicate question of refreshment was got over by
the hostess ; and what nwans were used to keep sixty
infants from parti-coloured convulsions and free fights
would make interesting reading if I only knew,
I am sorry to hear that the health of the gentleman
who was the innocent cause of the late Lord Ancram’s
death has become affected in a most lamentable way.
Yet another charity hop on the tapestry. A big hall
(as the inaugm-ators hope) is to bo hold some time in Sep-
tember, in Sydney Town Hall, in aid of the Boys’ Brigade
—a deserving aggregate which keeps its eye on the gamins
and poor little street fry. There is an influential com-
mittee, and Mr. A. Kirkcaldic is lion. sec. Tickets, 12s. Od.
Mr. and Madame Charhonnet-Kellerman gave a musical
“at home” to meet Commander Poydehot, of the Armand
Behic, on 20th inst., and although the lion failed to roar
as his hark was still on the sea, regrets were forgotten
when the music began.
I am told in two private letters that one of the curiosities
at Lady Salisbury's last reception in London was the wife
of a Victorian official who wore the most extraordinary
gown that was ever seen at such a function. It must
have been made in the Imckhlooks, and it attracted as
much attention as if the wearer had just come out of the
Ark.

A wildly grotesque affair is “Skipped by
the Light of the Moon,” which blazed up at
Sydney Opera House on Saturday last, and is
still blazing. It is partly comedy, partly farce,
partly delirious burlesque, partly pantomime, with
a certain amount of song-and-dance business
thrown in ; also, it is everything by turns and
nothing long, and the audience laughs all the
while, until its frock gives way and rips down the
back. It would have been a good piece for the
original Gaiety Co, to tackle, if Fred. Leslie had
got over the wall and barked to represent tho
bulldog. Included in it are two giddy hypo-
crites, who are supposed to be attending a
Y.M.C. A. conference, and who send the photo, of a
half-dressed ballet-girl to their wives as a proof of
their bona-fides, and only find out the mistake
when they try to gloat over the portrait of a fully-
dressed preacher, whereupon they rob the mail to
get back the compromising epistle, and, instead of
getting it, steal another man’s letter with a remit-
tance in it, whereupon they are chased by a
Scotch policeman with an Irish accent, and,
climbing hastily over a wall, they fall into the
mouth of a large dog, and, climbing back again,
they nearly fall upon their wives. Also, there are
the two wives who leave an infant for a moment
with a servant, who hands it to another girl, who
gives it to a man to hold for her, who deposits it
with various other people, so that at last it vanishes
altogether, till a man is seen falling out of a win-
dow with it, and a policeman catches it, whereby a
detective gets a black-eye, and the rheumatic foot
of a certain heavy father swells up till it obscures
the landscape, in consequence of which he isn’t
able to run after Lis daughter’s lover with a huge
stick so fast as be wants to, and that individual
COUNTESS OF ORKNEY (CONNIE GILCHRIST).
escapes until the third act, when two infuriated
women arrest him by the hair. Further, there is
a distracted yohng man who isn’t himself, but
somebody else, and an ungrateful daughter in a
pale-green frock, and a detective who never
detects anything, and a mother weeping over her
lost child, and an officer slinking around in search
of beer and pie, and these individuals fall reck-
lessly off roofs and out of high windows like dis-
tracted indiarubber monkeys bounding in a forest
of steel springs and gutta-percha.
# # #
The principal monkey in the crowd is that
limber, boneless comedian John Gourlay, who
seems to be made of gristle and wire. The other
good young man who gloats over the unclothed
ballet-girl is played by George Walton, and the
success is principally owing to this pair who con-
duct themselves like two snufflebuaters possessed
with a devil. Mrs. Bennett, as the nice little girl
who is embraced by Mr. Jewett, gives a better
performance than anything she lias yet done.
Mr. Grevillo—the swelled father with rheumatism
and Mr. Shine arc also at their best, only
why the latter should play Felice man MTntyre
with an Irish accent and a flaring red head
passeth all understanding. Also, Mrs. Buyer
makes ns think sadly of poor, dead Maggie Oliver,
who used to play tho ponderous, damp, distracted
woman with the baby and tho red shawl and the
hysterics in a way that would make a flat-iron
weep. Tho rest Of the company is reasonably
good, and tho scenery is all that can bo desired,
particularly the view of the ocean in the first act.
This paper has always regarded the ocean as a
deserving institution which is worthy of support,
and we are glad to put in a good word for it when
(he opportunity presents itself.

Jennie Lee’s benefit at Sydney Opera
House was a stragglesome affair which had most
of the symptoms mentioned in the liver-nip
advertisements and the porous-plaster announce
ments. It was a very long show, and con
seqnently it got jammed towards the end like a
giraffe trying to get round a narrow corner, and
there were too many items in it, and some of the
items travelled very heavily for want of being-
greased. Moreover, the house was crowded, and
the pit, which had evidently been drinking,’tried
to make a beast of itself towards eleven o’clock
and succeeded even better than might have been
expected—in fact, it made eight distinct beasts if
not more. The first act of “The Grasshopper”
conducted principally by Miss Jennie Lee and
Messrs. Poulton, Lash Gordon, Jewett, and Shine
was a real success, and following that came the
first act of “Jane,” in which Miss Myra Kemble
brought down the house with acclamation. Then
Miss Albu sang " ‘ KiHarney ” in a vigorous voice
and after that things began to flounder heavily’
and occasional thuds could be heard as if the
actors were killing each other behind the scenes
Jennie Lee’s new song was hampered a good deai
by the fact that she hadn’t had time to learn the
words; and a little sketch entitled “A Happy
Pair” came to grief in the pit. Miss Alice Per-
gusson, an amateur of considerable ability, and
Mr. Lash Gordon had the stage to themselves in
this item, and the lady was not so audible as she
might have been, and the yells and vociferations
of the pit and its demands that she should
“speak up ’’—which she didn’t—reduced the house
to something approaching chaos. Finally
Miss Jennie Lee came before the curtain
in a flushed and excited state as if she had
just executed Miss Fergusson and the unburied
remains of that lady were weighing heavily upon
her soul, and was presented with a wreath. She
also thanked the audience, and made a few pointed
observations about the pit, which was an obvious
waste of time ; she ought simply to have come out
with a shot-gun and emptied it at the shilling
seats on general principles. After that the last
items on the programme were successfully omitted,
with the exception of a scene
from “Jo”—which would
have been a triumphal con-
clusion, only most of the
dress-circle was gone by that
time, partly owing to the
lateness of the hour, and
partly for fear the pit should
break out and rend tl em
asunder-and the proceedings
ended far on in {he night,
when the hotels were shut
and no one could get a
drink for love or money,
except at a place in Efskine-
street, wherea belated nigger
was selling squash on a liar-
row.

Carl Hertz, the magician,
at Sydney Garrick, has
been conducting himself
this week in an even more
unaccountable manner than
he did the week before,
and weak-minded peoi le wf o
witness Ins demoniac con-
duct generally fly in affright
under an impression that
tlieyareseeingvisions. Appa-
rently heislettinghimselfout
theconcluding nightsofhisia
engagement, and now no man
in the audience feels safe lest
Hertz should put a skeleton
in his hat, and no woman
can feel quite sure that seven
devils won’t fall out of her
back hair. His two great
illusions—the person who
walks in the air, and the
man who is chained down on
a board and then vanishes,
his place being taken by a
woman are still in full
bloom, and no one has yet
found out the mystery of
either of them. If Mr.
Hertz only knew i t
he is losing a great chance
to earn popularity and
undying gratitude-in Sydney. If he would dis-
card his assistant for one night, and send Mr.
Abigail up in the air instead of him, and make
that gentleman float there until he pays off tho
depositors of the Australian Banking Co. in full,
he would bring down the house ; and if, when the
chained-up gentleman vanishes off the board,
instead of a girl appearing in his place the
audience could find those two lost financiers, Bel
and Davis, fastened bv the leg to the plank and
struggling desperately to get away back to South
America, there would arise a roar of joy which
would blow the roof off the Garrick and bulge tho
walls out into the street.

* * *
rpi 10 Parker - Rignuld arrangement of
“ Faust,” which somehow suggests the skeleton of
('oethe’s poem tricked out in a fin de siecle suit
- glops, was produced at Melbourne Princess’ on
Saturday- Apart from a few ingenious novelties
i the stage-supernatural department, this revival
" f the Girl, the Flesh, and the Devil has nothing
to recommend it in preference to the orthodox
“Faust” which Hignold played at the Opera
House some six or seven years ago. The Devil
personally has degenerated in some respects. At
the outset, after popping up, piping hot, from the
Mace where most men go, he doesn’t come to the
point quick enough. He grows garrulous with
advancing years, apparently, and cuts the cheapest
kind of jokes with all the smug complacency of a
police magistrate. Also his bad leg is cronker
than it used to be. A lack of unearthliness is
noticeable in his serio-comic songs, and ho seems a
commonplace lump of infamy, as a rule. In fact,
there’s a general impression of Deeming about this
jocular Satan. However, notwithstanding that
he isn’t much of a King of Evil, nor the
least bit of a fallen angel, George Rignold
puts much strong work into his performance
raid iseffecti vein the limelight throughout the show.
Marguerite is naturally, tenderly, and, above all,
ver y youthfully presented by Miss Bessie
Rimiolcl. She loves, suffers and repents iu a style
that attracts the sympathy of every body in front,
an d her ascension to Heaven is a pleasing incident.
Old Faust fills the part of auditor to the Devil’s
ptrotistical orations iu a spirit of self-abnegation,
whilst Cosgrove, as young Faust, never appears to
be haunted by the recollection that lie was sud-
denly run up by contract. The Martha of Miss
Blanche Lewis is an aggravating uncertainty, and
several other people leave room for improvement.
Scenery and optical illusions, being now as they
were in Sydney, go far towards covering the
literary deficiencies of this new “ Faust.” Things
didn’t work quite right on Saturday, though, and
prior to Satan’s first appearance in a cloud of
steam his familiar voice had been heard from afar,
commanding some invisible minion to leave a
window open.

“ The Times,” despite its inherent bitter'
ness and confusion of purpose, is going well at the
Bijou. An idea that Brough’s discomfiture and
unpathetic collapse are bits of wholesome fun
seems to prevail, and a large proportion of the
multitude laugh at the misery of a chastened snob
simply because it is Brough who buries his face in
the sofa cushion. For such people the faults of
“The Times” lie under the surface and are not
matters for regret. Nevertheless, it is a good
play undoubtedly gone wrong. Pinero has come
out with several other plays which were striking
examples of how a brilliant dialogue writer, clever
delineator of character, and master of stage business
can go out of his way to build an inartistic story.
But this is exceptionally good. Moreover it
doesn’t go wrong until the end is near, and its wit
lias struck home. The retirement of Annie
Taylor, whose place is taken by Bessie Major,
hasn’t made the performance more excellent than
it was at first, though the action is a little more
brisk. Miss Marie Fraser introduces herself to
Melbourne notice iu the minor character of an
over-educated maiden who has crammed her weary
head with a chronic ache. She figures as the niece
of Miss Cazalet (Miss Romer) until in a moment
of unguarded emotion she calls the other spinster,
“mother!” “ Little fool!” says her bogus aunt,
“I knew you’d let that out one day.” . The
incident has no bearing on the plot. The author
throws iu the disclosure gratuitously, and its
humour is characteristic of his play.

Mrs. Bernard Becre bade her Melbourne
admirers adieu at the Opera House on Saturday,
a large mixed programme and a second farewell
demonstration being provided for the occasion,
Mrs. Beere and Co. will, it is understood, depart
for England very shortly, so there is little likeli-
hood of the. lady saying another goodbye at the
end of a third season. But you can never tell
what may happen to a travelling “star.” The
Gaiety Co. farewelled at the- same theatre only
three weeks ago, and on Saturday next (30th) they
will re-appear iu a new burlesque, “ Carmen up to
Date,” In the meantime, John F. Sheridan in-
tends to put iu three* more positively final appear-
ances'. Ho was supposed to have sailed for Europe,
or somewhere, quite a long while ago, yet his
hright smile lingers in our midst, and the agony of
tearing himself away is unduly pro’onged. John
E. will produce “Bridget O’Brien*’ on Wednes-
day, and make another dying speech on Friday,
ht the second act of this elastic comedy, a girl is
to be shot from a cannon on the stage, right into
the arms of a gentleman, who will hang from the
dome of the theatre by his eyelashes. None of
Sheridan's other farewells had a cannon iu them,

An air of depression and “paper” per-
vades the Alexandra, where the indomitable
Boothman and the dauntless Stark seem still to
cater for the missing million. They have now got
as far as “ East Lynne,” in wild belie r that the
million have been waiting round the corner for a
fair excuse to come forward and weep. Kalis,
Cyclorames, &c., are much the same as usual, and
the Circus has not gone right away, but merely
into the suburbs. The Ovido Musin Concert
Company make a first Australian appearance at
the Town Hall, on Saturday (30th). The Maori-
land notices of Musin’s fiddling indicate a
musical treat iu store.

One of the sights of C illins-sfcreet lately is a certain
very massive actress leading out her baby daughter—a
child of about four, dressed in skirts that terminate with-
out any visible beginning—with the evident purpose of
exposing to public wonder a pair of infantile legs of
Brohdignagian proportions. The child has the sympathy
of all who know her.
Alf. Massina, of Melbourne, who accompanied Bern-
hardt and troupe to San Francisco, says the story is all
fudge about her going up the rigging, and getting
drenched with the green sea wave. From her staid and
studious demeanor lie concludes that the stories of iter

One evening, just before the mail left, Sara Bernhardt
was expected to recite at London Lyric Club for some
charitable institution. When she arrived at the place
there was no one waiting co receive her but Mr. Monday,
the secretary. “Where are Messieurs the members of
the committee?” she asked. Monday commenced to
blunder out some apology, which apparently didn’t
satisfy her, for she suddenly discovered that she had for-
gotten her fan, or her powder-puff, or something, and
incontinently departed, not to return.
George Darrell (says our London correspondent) has
been offered, and has refused, two engagements, for good
and sufficient reasons. He talks of touring with the
“ Sunny South,” which he thinks will go well in the pro-
vinces. It is not unlikely that Penley may visit Australia
under his auspices, to play a round of the characters
which have made his name as England's cleverest eccen-
tric comedian.

Clement Scott, D.T. critic, feeds cockney vanity with
such taffy as this:—“Sarah Bernhardt loves a London
audience. She may he careless, indifferent, wilful, or
mutinous elsewhere —seldom hero, and never on a first
night. It is an historical fact that the ‘divine Sara’
never plays so well as on a first night in London.” All
of which is bosh. J. C. Williamson, who has had a far
wider experience of Sara’s acting than the oracle of Fleet-
street, said that her first performance in Australia was
the .finest thing he ever saw her accomplish, whilst Sara
herself has expressed a dislike for London audiences, and
says that, outside of Paris, she scores her greatest Euro-
pean triumphs in Amsterdam. So there, now! Sara
miy not lie as mutinous in London as she was in Mel-
bourne. Cleopatra, according to the D.T. notice, “had
to act down (he laugh when a low comedian slave wriggled
on his stomach and turned the play into a farce.” Munie,
the low comedian referred to, provoked the same laugh
on the first night of “Cleopatra ” at Melbourne Princess’,
hut Sara didn’t content herself with acting it down. She
curled her lip at the house and said, “ O, enough !” in
choice English, and the house enoughed on tiie instant.
■Sara shut tlic laugh up, in fact.

Why did Marius not slick to the French stage? -Because
tiie wages are twice and three times as much in England,
and French artists of every rank are astonished to learn
what their compeers get over the Channel. A man as
good as Marius thinks £0 a week good. So many French-

Miss Jessie Middleton, daughter of the popular post
and telegraph master at Goulhurn, N.S. W., promises to
achieve some distinction as a pianiste. She recently per-
formed at a concert in Leipzig, and was warmly praised
for her technique. Miss Nettie Atkinson, the young
grand-daughter of General Aikinson (who lias several
limes visited Australia buying horses for the Indian
Government, and niece of Mrs. Pat. Osborne, of Curran-
dooly, Bungendore), lately gave, in London, a most suc-
cessful violin recital.
No greater frost, remarks Tim Bullsns correspondent,
has been known iu London than “Hero and Leander.”
Curly is like a boar with a sore head, and talks of the
decadence of English taste. The fact is that theatrical
business has been at a very low ebb for some time past,
and except at the Lyceum and the English Opera House,
where Bernhardt lias packed the house nightly, paper has
represented the slid! which makes the mare to go. Even
at the St. James “Lady Windermere’s Fan ” has ceased to
attract, and paying audiences have long been unknown.
The “New Wing,” a reemt success in England, is
intended to utterly squash Socialism—and this is how it
starts. The heroine is a General’s daughter - she believes
in Socialism. Her lover is a baronet—he doesn’t believe.
He resalves to show the girl that Socialism is a mistake.
Therefore he disguises himself as a workman, and he and
another man corns to paper the house. The real work-
man is coarse, red-faced, pimpled, beery, and smells of
short pipes, and he spits on the carpet. The baronet
doesn’t spit. After they have papered the house the girl
realises that Socialism is all wrong, and she marries the
baronet, and when the curtain comes down a nefarious
democratic theory is understood to he squashed. Either
(ho moral is that all Socialists spit on the carpet, or else
that only a gentleman can make a good paper-hanger —
no one knows which.
Out in the back blocks, the other day, a certain fat
person who plays female characters asked the waiter for
fowl. “ Fowl’s off, sir,” said the waiter. “Why is it
off?” enquired the fat person. “ Because there ain’t no
more,” replied the waiter. “What else kin I bring you,
sir? ” said the waiter at length. There were tears in the
bass voice as it replied : “ Oh, any darn ole tiling’ll do me.
Gimme some liver - leg of a ’possum —anything that no-
body else wants is good enough for me.”

Tiie London Star on “Hero and Leander” as done
by Mrs. Brown Bellew and Mr. Kyrle Potter :—O
Aphrodite, what things are done in thy name ! Such
things as “Hero and Leander,” O Aphrodite, at the
Shaftesbury, where they do persistently take thy name in
vain, hut where they do not know how to sp-II it. O
Aphrodite, who wast—l say “ wast,” O Aphrodite,
because they are very fond of “wast” and “ wouldst,”
and “hadst,” and other forms in “st” at the Shaftesbury
—who wast the goddess of loveliness, and who rose—l
mean rossst—from the sea, if thou couldst but look down
on the loveliness of Mrs. Brown-Potter and Miss Edith
Chester, and the other virgins of thy temple at the
Shaftesbury, and upon Mr. Kyrle Bellew rising from the
sea—from the patent linoleum waves of the many-
saunding sea, in fact, O, Aphrodite-why then I wonder-
wliat thou wouldst say. And then Mrs. Hero Brown-
Potter takes the veil, notwithstanding the protests of
Mr. Leander Kyrle Bellew, whose classic Greek limbs are
appropriately tattooed witli the flags of the British Mer-
cantile Marine, O Aphrodite, and the virgins of thy temple
do intone a hymn which is much like the hymns (ancient
and modern) chanted in the churches of ill > Christians.
Only there is no collection, O Aphrodite. Know further
that in the second act Hero hangs out her lamp, which
she calls a “ lah-ah-alunp,” as a beacon for her tattooed
adorer, who swims the Hellespont and yet comes out of
the water quite dry. Why, O Aphrodite? Because if he
had been wet, Hero would not have fallen into his arms,
as she does at the Shaftesbury. just as the (tantalising)
curtain descends on the second act. Know yet again
that in the third act thy virgins sing another hymn to
Eros (whose name they pronounce with a‘ long e and a
short o, O Aphrodit',) until they are driven away by the
High Priest and Dick Deadeye, who insist upon ex-
tinguishing Hero’s lah-ah-alunp, in order that Leander
may he drowned in the patent kamptulicon waves of
poluphloish —you know what I mean, O Aphrodite. And
when he is drowned Hero flings herself in after him, and
thy virgins sing the audience out with yet another hymn.
And the language of these Greeks is very nineteenth-
,century English, O Aphrodite, and their robes are garish,
and their dances are of the school of the sacred lamp—l
should say, la-ah-ahmp-and altogether this “Hero and
Leander” arrides me not, O Aphrodite.

Sydney Lyceum theatre rapidly approaches completion,
although the opening is fixed for Boxing Night. The
Pitt-strect front, which is chiefly hotel—the license for
it was granted by the Licensing Court last week—is
seven storeys high, the stage is CSfl. deep, and the seating
accommodation 1700. The house is to have two circles—
dress and family—and only two private boxes. William-
son and Musgrove are the lesscek. In all probability the
.opening piece will lie “The Mountebanks," with Nellie
Stewart in the lead.

We went to Ophelia’s funeral, and it was strange. Her
father happened to be Polonius, but her mother was Lady
Macbeth. In the front parlour we met the Kinchin,
Ingomar, Lady Teazle, Tony Lumpkin, MegMerHlees, and
much utility. They distributed iu the mourning coaches
and so to the cemetery, and round the grave. Very
peculiar the whole affair, to one accustomed merely to
the professionals on the stage, and one thought on how
Cervantes anticipated, or accompanied Shakespeare, by
writing, “ Life is as a piny, where the performers 1 all un-
robe when the curtain falls.”
