After 55 days of court-sitting, the big
Mortgage-M'llwraith, &c. case, has been con-
cluded in Brisbane, Q. Chief Justice Lilley
reserved judgment. Whichever way His Honor
rules, an appeal will be made to the Full Court of
Queensland, and whichever way the Full Court
decides, an appeal will be made to the British
Privy Council.
By 40 votes to 4, Queensland Legislative
Assembly has passed M. B. Gannon’s motion :
“That no Judge shall sit alone in court or
chambers in any matter in which his son acts as
counsel.” This is a flank movement against Chief
Justice Lilley, and his son Edwin, who, for some
reason or other, gets most of the briefs in cases
that are beard in his father’s court. Ten to one
is a tolerably decided majority ; but then, as
Morgan, M,P., remarked, at present it is possible
for one member of a family (evidently referring to
Charles Lilley, of Lilley and O’Sullivan, solicitors)
to be instructed in a case ; for that member to brief
another member of the same family (Brother
Ned); and for the case to be heard before a third
member of the family (the dad of both). The
Noes to the motion were—Sir S. W. Griffith,
g.C., Hodgkinson, Sayers and Hoolan. Barrister
rake walked outside the bar and declined to
vote. The only lawyer who voted boldly for the
motion was Charley Powers, M.P. for the
Burmin. The rest of the devil’s contingent were,
according to the division-list, carefully absent.
Judging from a number of apologetic and
semi-apologetic statements in the daily papers
there is just one redeeming feature about that
miserable proceeding whereby soi-disant Repub-
lican Premier Dibbs sunk into knighthood—he
refused the “honour” when it was first offered
him, and only succumbed to a “command ” from
the Queen conveyed in her own royal handwriting.
Iu other words, the woman tempted Dibbs and lie
did become a K.C.M.G. What a classical picture
it would make - the fall of Dibbs, if done after
the manner of the sketches in the good, little
Sunday-school books, where a naked Adam, partly
concealed by the branch of a tree, falls through
the temptation of an Eve mid nodings on, modestly
hidden behind a shrub. Also what a long anil
bony Adam Dibbs would make roaming in a
state of nature through the bovvers of Paradise-
on-the-Solent, and what an unspeakable row there
would be about Eve when she was published !
But, to come down to ordinary prose, and view-
ing Dibbs in a commonplace aspect with his
clothes on, the excuse about the royal “command”
is altogether too thin. If Victoria had invited
Dibbs to be hanged for her special amusement
and satisfaction, or to become a Eoreign-trader
(which would be just as reasonable as asking
him to change from Republicanism to Im-
perialism), or if she had asked him to elope with
an aged widow, or to commit suicide, he would
probably have refused, and if a royal “command”
isn’t binding iu one instance it is not in the
other. The one excuse that can be offered for the
fallen Republican is that lie is a man with tin
unusually large number of female relatives, and
possibly be lias been bowled out so often
when he tried to stand his ground against a lady
that ho lias given up making the attempt, and
simply knocked under from force of habit.
“ ’Tis not in mortals to command success,
but we’ll do more, Sempronius—we’ll deserve it.”
Something of this sort must have crossed our
readers' minds on learning that Dibbs had been
knighted. Sensible men of all shades of opinion
can have but one feeling for the man who poses
as a Republican and greedily clutches a nick-
name when be gets the chance. When our
“ Longfellow ” returns, the bauble is to be kept in
the Sydney Safe Deposit.*

There is trouble in the British royal family
just now over a point of etiquette. Eight years
ago the Duke of Albany died and left a vacant
chair. He was a thin, feeble youth with fits, and
he had a skin that was so thin that he was in
danger of bursting every day after dinner; also
he was religious in a depressed and wretched sort
of way; and, finally, he went on a very small
spree, and after a glass of ginger ale and a cigarette
he fell down some steps and died. His widow has
worn weeds and lived in strict seclusion ever since,
but at last she grew tired of feeding on great
hunks of melancholy, and having cold grief every
day for dinner. But Victoria put her foot down,
and declared that the bereaved lady must remain
bathed in woe and shed tears in a dark room till
the day of her death, and in the end the daughter-
in-law rose in insurrection, and banged her mis-
shapen crape bonnet on the floor, or something to
that effect,.', afid refused to go weeping over
Albany’s tomb any m.qre, and at latest advices
she had been exiled to Germany, where she will
probably reihain.

Aboriginals, for some reason not easily
understood, do not like Germans. It is related
that on one occasion a nigger who came down
from the North East to Terowie (S.A.) was asked
how many shearers there were on a certain station.
He replied, “Ten white men and three (blanky)
Germans! ”
At Broadwater on the Richmond (N.S. W.),
at the commencement of the sugar-season, there
wore 200 more applicants for work than could be
taken on. “ Those rascally unemployed stick to
the towns and refuse to go out into the country
where there is plenty of work to be had for the
asking.”
White men have undertaken cane-cutting
contracts at Bingera, Q. “But white men can't
cut cane, don’tcherknow,” cry the planters.
Naoraji (the dark man just elected to the
Commons) is not a Hindoo, but aParsee, a race as
distinct from the bulk of Indians, both Hindoo
and Mahometan, as are the English.

Domino.
I SUPPOSE that Domino means Master,* in soni o
sense or another. For instance, when the slan?.
whanging billiard-marker says “Domino” instead
of “game” he means that one has conquered the
other. This puts me in mind that gaming is t OQ
serious to be considered a game, when there j s
money on it. If I pass at euchre I say “pass.”
Tommy the Nut and the Derwent Nugget, eth'oc
genus omne, thump the table, or say “Round ”
“Away,” “Traveller,” “Off it,” &0., and there-
fore I don’t play with them. I plead guilty
however, to “Pop” for shout, instead of “Jq
propose ” at ecarte —who plays ecarte in the bush ’
But poor old Domino said that word in answer to
almost any demand or <%t the completion of any
job. If you asked him to bring round your horse
“Domino.” When you tipped him a shilling on
mounting, “Domino.” At'the end of each of the
two pints in which he invested that coin, “f) 0 .
mino.” When he closed the stable-door, “Domino.”
His grace after meals, the identical trisyllable.
Having lit the front lamp, as usual. When he
left for bed, his Are, just the same. His transla-
tion of Bravo or Encore resulted in the never-ending
expression. Therefore, he got the name of
Domino. No one during his life at the Arch ever
knew his other name. He could read and write
but at his quarterly settling he just signed an
oblong: with twelve dots round the edge—six on
each side. He was groom and rouseabout at the
Marble Arch, a public-house in the Saltbush
district. I knew him for years, and I knew no
wrong of him. He was always clean and neat •
seldom quite sober, never insensibly drunk. His
room was the picture of tidiness, and he had some
very good clothes. He had a secret, which I dis-
covered by chance. And it was love. The publi-
can’s daughter was just the beginning and the
end of his adoration. When the blacks were
about, Domino camped on the verandah near
Prudie’s door. There was never any doubt about
the girths or reins on Pru.’s hack when she rode
to church, twelve miles, once a month. The
buggy and harness were clean and shiny when Miss
Maccaferi went to the Baranna Show or the Shan-
dandera races, although they might be a bit
spotty when old Maccaferi and the Countess
wanted them for a drive. Domino was not old
and was not bad-looking; and I fancy Pru. had a
sort of penchant for him. She did lead him a
dance. It was her business to milk and attend to
the dairy. Still Domino bailed up the cows,
carried the buckets, scalded the dishes, scrubbed
the slabs and floor, turned the churn, and washed
it after use. All this in addition to his other
duties, and all for love of Miss Prudencia. As I
said, he spent his wages and was constant at his
post. _ Once a year, however, he went to Shandair-
dera in the coach for some private business which
no one inquired into. Macca. said it could not be
for a spree, because he only took a pound and
brought back some shillings. His passage was
free, because he looked after the mail horses. One
day he was after a heifer which was to be broken
in, when he nissed the coach and asked me for a
lift to the town. I was only too glad to take him
for company, and I heard the eternal “Domino”
each time he mounted after a gate. Then he let
out that he was going to make his will and leave
it in the bank. It seems he was not well and had
been depositing some yearly sum from home
without touching it. I went with him and we
drew out a simple will leaving his all to Prudence
Maccaferi, of the Marble Arch, near Shandandera,
N.S.W. Nevermind the amount, and don’t ask
for his name. He was back home next day. Then
came the breaking of the heifer. The latter broke
“Domino.” When his back was turned the
wretch rushed him and horned him. Prudie had
climbed up the fence. Poor old Dom held on to
the bale and just said one word, “Domino,”and
died as he had lived, at the feet of love.
There was a Frenchman at the funeral who told
me what I already knew, that the hospital argot
in France for a loaded coffin is a “Box of
Dominoes. ”
HIRMAN ABIFF.
Says G. H. Reid, M.P.
If we are to have freetrade in flesh and blood, as the
manufacturers wish, then we should also have freetrade
in the necessaries of life.
Topsy-turvy, as usual. We want Protection both
of our manufactures and of our flesh and blood—
i.c., our labour. Protected industries and “white”
wages.
In Queensland lately an heiress has been
found who is entitled under the will of one Mark
Gallagher, of New York, to a great part of his
fortune—about .£30,000 in cash and an interest in
much property in the city of New York. All
papers in connection with the case have been sent
across the pond by Mr. G. E. R. Jones, of Sydney,
solicitor, who has charge of the case, and is certain
of being able to make good his client’s title.
Christie Murray’s embroglio with the com-
mittee of the Savage Club ended in a compromise.
The attendance at the meeting called to discuss
the matter was so great that the club-house could
not accommodate all, Murray, in arraigning the
committee for their censure, made an eloquent
speech. He somewhat sarcastically admitted that
he had committed a technical mistake in not more
carefully editing the list of those to whom he
addressed his circular on behalf of Odell, and he
made a great hit when he asked what the big
literary men who formed that original “circle of
brains and brotherhood called the Savage Club
would say were it possible for them to know that
a Savage had been accused of impropriety m
making an appeal to his fellow-members on behalf
of a distressed brother. Several members then
hinted at the propriety of some kind of compro-
mise, and eventually it was decided amid applause
that while the meeting was of opinion that the
committee had not acted ultra vires in taking the
matter up as they had done, the resolution censur-
ing Murray should be expunged from the minute-
book.

for years Griffith’s colleague in the representation

We have got at the helm a man who believes in nothing

The latest capitalistic idea in Bananaland
is to treat with the Indian Government for the
indentation of coolies unlimited, with their wives
and families, each old-man coolie to be paid the
handsome salary of £l3 a-year and family rations,
besides one turban, one loin-wrap and one suit of
pyjamas ; the labour of the wives and children to
be thrown in.
The Griffilwraith Ministry are seriously
contemplating the absolute repeal of the Payment
of Members Bill. Politics are a matter of'bread
and butter to a good few of the sycophantic
followers of the coalition, but allowing even for
the secession of these The Bulletin has every
reason to believe that this crowning act of infamy
will be performed.

Although Maoriland Parliament’s protest
against the Queensland Kanaka slave-trade revival
is regarded in the quarter at which it is aimed as
the impertinent interference of an outsider, yet
there is a moral satisfaction in knowing that it
was embodied in a Government resolution and was
carried by a majority of 34 to 18.
John Donaldson, 1 lie suspected leader of
a third political party in Queensland, is too weak-
fibred to be relied on. The sort of man who
professes teetotalism as a virtue and an example,
and invests in brewery shares as a source of profit,
lias ioo many friends all-round to be ever dis-
tinctively useful to any one party.
Randal, the Queensland emigration lec-
turer, is still, despite Griffith’s solemn assurance
that the emigration policy is abolished, holding
forth in England about the advantages of the
“ village settlement scheme,’.’ and is imploring
the British Hodge to come .out and take up the
land which was supposed to be opened for the
benefit of the unemployed population. Verily the
average emigration lecturer would hold forth
about the bright green verdure in Sheol, and the
water-frontage allotments along the blackened
shores of the Styx, and the pastures which'would
support two horned devils to the. acre.

A committee of investigation appointed by
the U.S. Congress reports, after taking a great
mass of evidence, that the cost of living has been
reduced by 3 per cent, since the M‘Kinley Act
came into force, through the all round diminution
of prices. This cablegram excited much deep
silence among the Freetrade papers of Sydney.
Dibbs left N.S. W. as “ The only Republi-
can Australian Premier who has ever visited
England.” This was his own boast. A man of
that sort had to be squelched. He accepted a
Knighthood at the autograph invitation of Queen
Victoria, who stepped down from the throne to
pull the “Republican Premier’s ” teeth.
Poor little Hodgkin son, the Minister for
Mines (Q.) and member for the Burke, is intro-
ducing a Bill to halve his electorate, which he
isn’t game to go within a hundred miles of. He
intends to cut out the “ proputty ” half for him-
self, and when the general elections come round to
sit and poke fun across the fence at John Hoolan’s
hairy-toothed constituents, who have been wait-
ing to meet him up that way a long time now.
What are oaths worth, after all ? When
Napoleon was elected President of France, he
took a terrific oath of fidelity, saying : “ May this
right arm wither if it is ever raised against the
integrity of the Republic.” Then he overset the
Republic, and he didn’t wither, and when he died
after being a bogus Csesar for twenty years, it was
not his arm, but his stomach, that went wrong.
Barlow’s accepted amendment to the
Elections Bill lately before Queensland Parliament
provides for contingent voting, and is said to pre-
vent minorities returning members. It’s a com-
plicated puzzle, and disputes as to how it works
have already separated many old friends. One
result will be the further disfranchisement of the
worker, who, unable to elucidate the puzzle, will
neglect contingent voting altogether.

In connection with Judge Denniston’s
ruling that the Licensing Act of Maoriland was
not a prohibitionist measure, and that the Syden-
ham licensing committee acted illegally in neglect-
ing to consider the requirements of those who
wanted to purchase liquor in Canterbury, it is in-
structive to note that during the past year the
Canadian Temperance Act has been repealed by
the electors of the province of Ontario, while
throughout the 64 counties of the entire Dominion
in 33 only does the measure yet survive.
Five years’imprisonment and to be hanged
at the end of that time, is the sentence passed by
the Bulgarian courts on the alleged assassin (if
Boltcheff, Minister of Finance. There is a won-
derful ingenuity about the codes of the little rat-
trap States of Eastern Europe, and the things that
are done outside the limits of the eode are more
ingenious still. The leader of the Bulgarian
Opposition was arrested a while ago chiefly because
the Government majority was getting shaky, and
then the Premier called round and had him
stripped naked in his cell, and flogged himVwith a
large leather strap. He was then -officially tor-
tured by the hangman, and hung no bv the feet
off and on for over "ayear, and finally he'has been
sentenced to a long term in gaol. ' The Govern-
ment has commanded a steady majority ever since
then.

Says an American paper :
' The most devilish and effective attack yet made upon
the Single-tax movement was that of the ingenious man
; who bequeathed a large fortune to Henry George in a
contestible will. At last advices the great reformer,
broken in health and mind, bad spent the ultimate cent
of his life’s accumulations in defending the legacy, and
was borrowing money to sue his own lawyer for the brass
button that remains of it. During these proceedings the
Single-tax movement, left to the guidance of Mr. George’s
disciples, has been steered in 750 directions, and its trail
resembles a parallel of latitude through the centre of dis-
turbance in a Japanese earthquake.
Under the peculiar conditions of the
naturalisation laws in connection with the
exclusion from the Queensland slave trade
of aliens who have_ only been naturalised under
Australian provincial laws, a more grievous
disability exists when the legal points are dove-
tailed with others in Griffith’s new Workers’
Disfranchising Act. An alien who has been
naturalised in another province is, under this new
Act, still a foreigner in Queensland and is debarred
from exercising any vote at an election. One
Kruger, naturalised in N.S. W,, has been declared
incapable of voting by a Bundaberg (Q.) magi.
trate.
The Victorian Bible-in-schools league has
a fixed resolve to introduce the Irish national
senes of school-books into Australia, and it is
constantly howling for the adoption of these
admirable works.” This is sufficient evidence
that there is nothing Australian about the league
—it is a doddering importation and nothing more,
ibe books it is so anxious about are full of
rampant Protestantism and bog-trotting lore from
one end to the other. There is nothing in them
but facts afiout Macgillicuddy’s Reeks, and the
dead O’Briens and O’Tooles, and if they were
used for a few years the rising generation would
hardly know that there was such a place as
Australia, and would fancy that the outside world
was all Ireland.
According to a new Act which has just
come in force in W.A., any person absenting
himself from his “lawful master” without suffi-
cient notice is liable to be fined or imprisoned, and
after his line is paid or his term of imprisonment
is finished he will be returned to his “lawful
master ’by the authority of the law. This, it is
supposed, will obviate strikes, for every man who
goes on strike will be promptly arrested. There is
nothing in about the master who absents
himself from his employe without due notice, but
that doesn t matter —if there was anything the
aforesaid master would sit on the bench to hear
the case, and then promptly discharge himself
without a stain on his alleged character.

of the North and Central districtsis.astill stronger
ferment, and all that applies to his attempted
provincial compromise applies with greater force
to the more I rojan remedy. And Griffith’s scheme
scares the land-boomers and ring-fakirs of South
Queensland, because (they argue) “proputty will
depreciate in the South directly the North and
Central districts are not governed from Brisbane
i.e., as soon as trade is decentralised to its
natural geographical localities. The Griffilwraith
power depends chiefly on the support of Southern
speculators and boodlers.
The Review of Reviews upon Gladstone :
He has never, so far as I can remember, in the whole
course ot Ins unparalleled career, made a speech that
betrayed even a glimmering ray of sympathy with the
wrongs under which women labour. . . . At the
close of a long life, no statesman lias seemed so stolid and
so persistently blind to them. ... The explanation
is not difficult. Mr. Gladstone is a parliamentarian who
lives on the electoral plane; women are not on that plane,
t..eiefore women do not enter into the sphere within
which ms conception of justice applies. .. . If women
had had votes he would have been as keen to see their
wrongs as if they had been men ; but they had no votes,
and he would not listen. . . . Wherein lies the ex-
planation of this great mystery' ? Surely nowhere save in
the fact that personal rights are so inseparably linked
with political privilege that Mr. Gladstone could not
realise a wrong that was inflicted merely on a thinn with-
out a vote. J
This is the position of. the Griffilwraith
Land-giant Railway Bill up to date. Sixteen
clauses have been approved of. These provide
briefly that the poles of the proposed line shall be
determined, but the syndicate may construct the
line by whatever route it pleases#. The allotment
of the land-payment shall be by “alternative
choice,” which is a very different thing from alter,
native blocks. The mining rights go with the
blocks. A double payment in land and money is
to be the reward of the constructing company.
The area of the land blocks is unfixed, as is also
the minimum price of the land. There can no
longer be any doubt that the whole thing has been
cut and dried, and that the hugest job ever carried
out in Australia is now being perpetrated in the
Queensland Assembly.
The Maoriland Government runs a life-
insurance department of its own, and now it pro-
poses to take another step in the direction of
socialism by the compulsory insurance in that
department of all civil servants. The department
will take them in the lump as healthy lives, and if
a particularly unhealthy or diseased gentleman
has to be accepted now and then, the great mass
of business done will cover the loss. Every civil
servant will be retired at 60; if he dies before
that age he will get the amount of his policy ; and
if he survives it he can either take a fixed sum or
an annuity until death. The policies can’t bo
sold, neither can they be seized by creditors, or
transferred in any way. And while it is at work,
the Ballanco Government might as well enact
that all the policies issued by its office shall bo
unsaleable and unattachable by creditors in the
same way. The seizure of an insurance policy for
debt is about as barbarous as the old fashion of
seizing a family and selling it for the benefit of
the father’s creditors.

Society
Lord Carington, it is cabled, will possibly
oe Viceroy of Ireland. Ere he takes such office
he should certainly polish up his grammar, as they
speak English rather well in Dublin. For
instance, hung up in bootmaker Abbey’s window,
George-streot, .Sydney, is a note of recent date in
Carington’s own fist: “I shall be glad if you
would send me two pairs of- boots,” &c. The
Baron’s attorney should get this shocking exhibi-
tion taken down instanter.
# # »
At a certain vice-regal residence a little
stranger is expected shortly. Mrs. Jobkins, a
leading society woman, as vulgar as she is wealthy,
recently met the vice-regal hostess at a reception,
and blurted out, “Oh, madam, you must really
give the little dear a Haustralian name !” There
were about twenty people within earshot, and her
ladyship was so pleased.
# * »
Speaking of Darmont—his position in
Divine Sara’s esteem was “spotted” at a glance
by a Bulletin representative, even before the
white-skinned young Frenchman had appeared as
Marc Antony. On the night of Sara’s debut, at
Melbourne Princess’s, a tall, slim, poetical stranger
came up from the lower stage regions and watched
“ Camille” from the dress-circle staircase leading
thereto. When the eye of The Bulletin observer
had taken in this vision he applied to Phil
Greenwood for information concerning it, but
Williamson’s factotum didn’t yet know the
Adonis on the stairs by sight, or name—
couldn’t say whether he belonged to the French
company, in fact. “That must be Sara’s mash,”
suggested the seeker after truth. So it was. At
least it turned out to be the Marc Antony of nine
nights later. Love no doubt inspired this sudden
conviction on the unsupported evidence of the
senses. Sara had fascinated her 099th (or there-
abouts) journalist, and the unhappy victim of a
hopeless, distant attachment knew his favoured
rival by instinct. Whether this weird peculiar
incident accounted for Darmont’s threat to storm
The Bulletin office when he reached Sydney,
and beat the editor to death with the mangled
limbs of the office-staff, vvou'd seem to be a
matter for psychological conjecture.
* # *
There was a death lately in the family of
a very rich man living in N.S.W., and a friend,
hearing of the sad event, telegraphed to the
bereaved father telling him how deeply he sympa-
thised with his affliction. Then the bereaved
father rose up and wired back: “Many thanks
for kind sympathy. Collect.”
• # *
On a Western (Q ) station a little while
ago, one colonial-experiencer bit a piece out of
another’s ear. The former was an English parson’s
son, and the other had a place in the peerage.
They quarrelled because the parson’s son had
stolen the peer’s gin.
* # *
A single woman takes up a homestead
selection under the present law. Next to her
patch, an agreeable and eligible young bachelor
also takes up a selection. The neighbours become
friendly, and there is the usual result as between
fire and tow. Now : If the selectress marries her
neighbour, the law steps in and forfeits her
selection. If she only cohabits with him on the
hither side of the broomstick, the law looks on
approvingly and considers her a good settler and
one to be encouraged. Both she and the young
man keep their selections, and live in “a state of
sin.” The achievement of that result is one of the
chief and most glorious aims of the Queensland
homestead settlement law.

Per mail delivered in Sydney on Monday :
One of the most striking figures at the recent State-
concert at Buckingham Palace was j our Premier. Ho
looked realij' splendid in his official livery. He for some
time struck against wearing any uniform, but Lord
Knutsford persuaded him to do so, telling him it was p
matter of etiquette which must be observed. A man who
was there tells me that Dibbs was “the cynosure of
neighbouring eyes,” and more than one fair dame asked
that he might be introduced to them. He stood for some
time amongst the Royal Circle and engaged in a pro-
tracted conversation with the Princess Christian. It is
evident that an attack is being made by these swells upon
George’s republicanism, but, so far, lie is unchanged and
unspoiled.
And now !

Sir Arthur Hodgson, at the “ Queensland
dinner ” in London the other day, related that he
had very recently had a conversation on the burn-
ing black-labour question with Lord Cork, who,
after expressing some valuable opinions on the
subject, asked him “where Kanaka was?” as ho
could not find the name on any map.

“‘The Mullens family,’late of Sydney,”
writes a London correspondent of The Bulletin,
“are having a brand-new coat-of-arms designed
by the officials of* the Heralds’ College. I was
privileged to see a drawing of it a few days ago,

No descendant of any of William the Conqueror's
brigands can boast a finer.”

Mrs. Campsie Dalglish, widow of the Goul-
burn Brokenhillionaire, was married very quietly
at the Brompton Oratory on 18th June. The
bridegroom is Mr. William Bellasis, son of Mr.
Sergeant Bellasis, the celebrated lawyer. After
a continental honeymoon, they take up their
residence at Lulvvorth Castle, Dorsetshire, quite
a show-place in the county.

Dear Bulletin, — Touching that (alleged)
Bohemian Club in Brisbane. The members mostly
wear broadcloth and purple and fine linen, pay
cash for their liquor, and are eminently respect-
able all round. Rumour hath it ('si nonevero)
that a would-be member was pilled because his
boots were half-soled. The members are princi-
pally recruited from the (apparently) wealthy
merchants of the city, the daisy barristers
and . solicitors, and the Bench, with a re-
porter or two thrown in to add a flavour
of Bohemianism. As a matter of fact, the prin-
cipal object of this “ Bohemian ” club is to enable
Smith, the prosperous trader, who is going ’Ome,
to got into the Savage Club on his Brisbane-club
qualification. A specimen of their Bohemianism.
Eoli and Harley were invited to the house-dinner,
but Pechtosch was left out. He had once been
a humble music-teacher in Brisbane, and so could
not be received by the magnates of the club.
Foil’s health was drunk, and went at that; he
neither spoke nor sang, presumably because a
brother professional had been slighted.

Butcher Stamford, of Windsor, has secured
as tenants for “ Deeming Villa ” a nerveful young
married couple who pay a modest crown a week
rental. Stamford was nearly driven crazy after
the murder by people hunting him up and offering
to live in the house for nothing.

The programme of the new Inebriates’
Home at North Sydney sets forth that “the
efficacy of prayer will be relied npon ” for the
cure of patients. Also the Women’s Christian
Temperance Union and the Women’s Prayer
Union are taking a hand in the management.
After this, our faith in the Inebriates’ Home
goes right down to zero. If anybody connected
with the show can give us a single, well-authenti-
cated case where an inebriate or anybody else was
cured by prayer, we will eat the manager raw,
with his hat and boots on, as a proof of onr bona-
tides, and give all our savings to* the Orange
Lodge.

Dear Bulletin, — Do you know that a
remnant of the old “ Barrack-wall ” still stands in
York-streefc, Sydney—a fairly reliable bulwark
between the pomps and vanities of Petty’s Hotel
and the sober seclusion of St. Philip’s parsonage?
Clothed with ivy and crowned with broken bottles'
it is as intelligent, as picturesque, and far more
interesting as a relic of “The System’’than any
M.L.O. in N.S.W.’s recognised collection. When
the Upper House goes to the wall, this is the one it
should go to, standing, as it does, on the blurmd
and indistinct borderland between the spiritual
consolations of this world and those of the next
I am, &c., Little Cricket.

It is doubtful if the Victorian labour-bureau
has been advantageous to the Melbourne unem-
ployed. Certainly it has knocked all the demon-
stration out of the men, who now stand round in the
room, like sheep, watching for their numbers to go
up. Without either demonstration or agitation ,
their condition grows worse daily.

Judge Molesworth refuses to give young
women hotel-licences in Melbourne, because of
their lack of efficiency as “chuckers-out.” The
popular opinion is that woman has fulfilled her
mission when she gets a man into the pub. She can
leave him to fall out.
* # #
The police on Sunday “ licensing-duty ”
in Melbourne will do anything to get a conviction.
In a recent case it was sworn that the policeman
had his leg bandaged and his arm in a sling, and
implored the licensee “to give me a drink, for
Cod’s sake, as I’ve walked all the way from Mor-
dialloc.” And yet the publican was fined, though
the policeman admitted the bandaging. Con-
stables who get convictions under such circum-
stances should be fined themselves.
# # #
Editor Bulletin, —Is it not time oceps
were taken to prevent resident magistrates from
being officially wedded for worse and worse and
for ever and ever to one district ? The further
you get away from the metropolis the greater the
stipendiary magistrate’s power becomes, till in
some parts he is a small tin god, whose rule
becomes very oppressive indeed. After a time he
becomes a big landowner, and a ring—or “ push”
—is formed of which he is the nucleus. He
is president of the local land-board, licensing
board, &c., &c., and as he, as a rule, is made on
the same system as other mortals, it cannot pos-
sibly be expected that by degrees he will not
become biassed in his rulings. Nor has the
private individual who suffers under his prejudice
any remedy, for it is one thing to prove to the
common mind that a flagrant injustice has been
committed from a moral point of view, and
quite another and more difficult to prove that the
same is the case from a legal standpoint. This
system of taking root in a place is also unfair to
the magistrate himself. It is time the authorities
take upon themselves the divine duty of leading
resident magistrates out of temptation by remov-
ing them —forcibly if they will not go willingly—
to a new district, say, every six years.—Toby.

The Dentists’ Bill aims chiefly at making
people pay ss. to a diplomee for having an ivory
dragged out, instead of a modest shilling, or half-
crown to a chemist, or horse-doctor, who has no
dental diploma, but can uncork jaws all the same.

quondam Australian actress. Plain Bill Holloway
is generally one of the party on these cruises.

OUT OP THE DEPTHS.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
On voiceless hearts in breasts unknown.
Somewhere, somewhere,
There must be love for thee alone,
Love free from care ;
There must be love, I know, I know,
Somewhere, around, above, below.
Ah, me ! but where S
Somewhere there must be ears to heed,
Somewhere, somewhere,
Somewhere there must he life indeed—
And love is there ;
Somewhere are calm and peaceful days,
Where life shall roll in restful ways.
Ah, me! but where ?
Oh ! you that come and pass and go,
Somewhere, somewhere,
Ye know the thing that we would know —
What hope is there.
What hope is there when faith has fled,

What hope is there ?

A well known doctor asserts that there
will bo plenty of small-pox in Sydney, shortly.
The disease, ho says, has had too free a leg since
the arrival of the Oroya to admit of its disappear-
ing in a hurry.
* * *
It is not necessary in order that the
praises of St Vincent’s Hospital, Sydney, should
be sung that the local Prince Alfred surgical staff
should be blackguarded:—“lt has been openly
stated that in Prince Alfred and to a less
degree in Sydney Hospital —no patients are
welcome unless they are willing to surrender up
part of their anatomy to feed the hospital eats or
instruct the hospital hangers-on in the art of dis-
section.” The Bulletin has nothing whatever to
say against St. Vincent’s Hospital as an institution,
but, until, like the other hospitals, it places
itself under the Hospitals Act and submits to
State inspection it should get no money out of the
State Treasury.

London Graphic has a picture of the
unemployed, “who won’t work,” lying about in
Melbourne Treasury Gardens —a venomous lie,
though drawn on the spot by a lady.

Gov. Kintore works his vice-regal staff at
a cost of about £l5OO a year, the major portion of
which is paid by Government. With two excep-
tions, the staff are all Scotch.
* * *
Among the S.A. Civil Service defaulters
still at large are Wood, the Public Trustee, who
vanished with his mistress and £ll,OOO, and is
now alleged to be no small potatoes in Mexico,
The absconding Lands Titles accountant and
Anglican lay-preacher, whom the Adelaide police
obviously don’t want to catch, is now in America,
and the ex-police-court clerk who has just
decamped is supposed to be at sea. As to the
defaulter caught in Sydney last week, and about
whose case, the public talked so much that a war-
rant had to be issued, it was only his own foolish-
ness that caused his arrest.
* # #
A prominent social light in Adelaide was
recently summoned for alleged brutal illtreatment
of a state child licensed to her. The case was to
have come before the police-court, but on the
morning of trial it was announced that owing to
the great pain that might be caused the accused
“a fine of £5 had been accepted by the Secretary
of the State Children’s Council ’’—and proceedings
were abandoned !
• • #
The cablegram announces that the young
Earl of Orkney has married that charming little
innocent, Connie Gilchrist. Some 15 years ago
the Gilchrist family, consisting of four sisters—
Sally, Tilly, Polly and Connie—were well-known
in London art circles as nude models who were
thoroughly up in their work. They all had excel-
lent figures, they had been virtually born in the
business and were without a vestige of mock
modesty. At that time Connie was a sharp,
bright little girl, a spoilt child of the studios,
and her portrait in the costume of Eve before the
arrival of the serpent is to'be found in several
pictures of Leighton, Poynter, and other well-
known painters. A good many of the London
models find employment in the off season as ballet
girls. Connie early learned to dance, and her
performances at some highly Bohemian entertain-
ments at a third-rate suburban music-hall arrested
the attention of John Hollingshed, ever on the
look out for oil for the sacred lamp. The success
she attained at the Gaiety was immediate and
phenomenal. Her skipping-rope dance brought the
shallow-pated Johnnies to her feet and she was not
slow to exercise her powei’. In what lay her
fascination it w'ould not be easy to determine. She
was not pretty, she had no voice, and she took so
little interest in her art that she could never have
become an actress, even had she possessed any
ambition in that direction. But Connie had bee
brought up in a good school for discovering the
faults and foibles of human nature; she had
listened to the talk of clever men and had realised
how easy it is to separate a fool from his money.
And now she has reached the end of the second
volume of her history by marrying an earl, and
will be henceforth known to the world as the
Countess of Orkney. To emphasise her triumph
over the passions of her admirers, the bride com-
pelled (for it can scarcely be imagined that any
sane person would do it of his own free will) her
former “protector ” to give her away at the altar
—a dramatic conclusion to a realistic romance.

Lord Hopetoun candidly admits that his
interest in the Charity Organisation Society
springs “ from perhaps a selfish motive.” “He is
one of those people, ’ he believes, “ who are sup-
posed to have the power to give.” Consequently,
it convenient to give a fiver a year to the
C. 0.5., so that when the lone, lorn widows come
along lie can give them a sympathetic smile and
send em off to Jacob Goldstein.

WOMAN AND HER MASTER,
(For The Bulletin.)
Injustice old—the timid and the meek
Downtrodden by the brazen and the strong.
Who, boastful, crush the gentle and the weak
With iron hand and iron heel as long
As she neglects to use her tongue and speak
For her own cause, and sounds the wrong:
Yes, let poor, tongueless woman, Phoenix-like, arise,
And on the ashes of her wrongs win rights and liberties.

Sydney as a barber ? She could make a fortune ?
* * #
A pert Salvation lass recently visited
Sydney Lands office on a begging expedition. The
first two men she met said, “ VVe don’t know any-
thing of the Army, but each of us will give you
half-a-erown ; ” and one giver proceeded to jot
down his initials on the list. “Hold!” quoth
the lass, “God willing, one only of you shall
sign and for five shillings; if you head the list
with half-a-crown a piece none will give more;
toss up, friends, to decide.”
* * #
“ Jersey Pudding,” judged by a sample in
a gilded Sydney restaurant, is mere plain bread-
pudding with a cockroach in it.
The Queensland Investment cases
will absolutely ruin several of the defen-
dants, especially if, as is threatened, the
Court on its own motion goes behind the
titles of the land concerned in the suit and
proceeds to enquire whether the deeds
aren’t void on grounds of dummyism. This
law-suit is the most murderous and
unending ever fought in Australasia.
ELFIR : “Ma says Mr. Johnson’s a regular ladies’ man. What’s a ladies’ man, papa?”
PAPA (lazy, and not jenlous): “ A lady’s maid without salary or petticoats.”
A Queensland civil-servant, when ques-
tioned the other day as to an applicant’s capa-
bilities, replied that he knew nothing of him
except that he had the largest nose in the district
from which he came. This same official was once
asked by his Department to furnish a schedule of
the several duties performed by him and the pay-
ment he received for each. He responded with a
list occupying two sheets of foolscap, and on the
second sheet under the heading “Payment” he
wrote “For the love of God.”.
* # *
Eighteen months ago, eight leading com-
mercial travellers, “ doing ” the Queensland coast,
entered into a compact, which was duly signed
and made legal. Each member, on the first of
every month, was to place <£s to a joint account
opened in a local bank, the deposits to be left
standing until April, '93, when the existing mem-
bers were to draw the proceeds and take a trip to
Chicago Exhibition ; any member not paying up
to forfeit his right to any part of the sum total.
Last month two of them dropped out, and now
three of the remainder find that if they leave their
places will be filled up, and they also intend to
forfeit. At present there is £640 to the credit of
those still in. If all but one dropped out, how
that bagman would enjoy himself 1

A Mudgee (N.S.W.) woman saw a snake
swallow a mouse, and courageously chopped the
reptile in two with an axe. The mouse ran out
unhurt, and the woman jumped on a fence and
shrieked pitiably for assistance until the mouse
was well out of sight.
A fragment from Jos. Symes ;
He was a well - dressed man of parsonic appear-
anee, and seemingly ia trouble about something, lie
accosted me as 1 came up with, “Sir! are you pre-
Pared?’’ “ For what?” I inquired surprisedly. He drew
a long breath, and then, with preternatural solemnity,
replied, “For death! Are you prepared to die ?” “Not
without a struggle,” I replied; “if you contemplate
murder, you must light for it.”
Andy Kelly, M.P. : As a member of the
committee for the relief of the unemployed it had
been his lot to see the result of an appeal to thebanks
for aid. One Sydney bank returned its list with
“one shilling” marked on it. (A Voice Send it
back.”) Another bank simply wrote across the
sheet, “lOU one penny,” and gave them one.
(Laughter.) The sooner the present Government,
or any other Government, awoke to the necessity
of having a national bank the better.
The railway from Culcairn to Corowa
(N.S.W.) is to be opened on 10th August next
for the Corowa Show. The line is a useless one,
passes through almost unoccupied country, and
& 1 ■”
will not pay grease for the wheels. It is 47J miles
long, and cost somewhere about =£103,000. There
is only one town on the whole length—that is
Corowa, and Corowa does all its business with
Melbourne. Culcairn is only a pub.

Finding a Father.
(F OR THE BULLETIN.)
, Tl ie y were sluicing an old bed of sand
norn the primitive battery of the ancient Star,
ior the sake of the pyrites and the fine gold, which
Latter had run over the ill-silvered plates of tl o
original tin-pot mill as freely as it had gathered in
tlie boxes and the ripples. Young Sam Bates
oversaw the job, a position awarded him by the
management in accordance with the understand-
ing that he and his brother were always to have
hist show when anything better than ordinary
was available about the mine.
Years ago the directorate gravely recorded
amongst the minutes of the company its resolution
to see the widow and orphans of Andrew Batts,
the first underground manager of the mine, well
provided for. They had honourably upheld this
resolve by making the boys work hard for their
living on top and below, firstly as battery-feeders,
then, when their muscles developed, as truckers,
and eventually on the brace and in the face.
Andrew Bates was the discoverer of the reef,
but iiad lost all interest in it in the mysterious
process of floating, and lived to see strangers grow
wealthy out of the find that yielded him bare
wages. It was only presumed that Mrs. Bates
was a widow and her children orphans, but the
presumption was rational as Bates mysteriously
disappeared one wild night when there was a big
flood in the gully, and the country for miles
around was mangled by a storm that is historical
in the district. The manager left home one after-
noon some years previous to the time of this story,
and struck out across Mount Bluff for the nearest
station, expecting to reach town by the last train.
He had been called up to attend a special meeting
of his creditors. Bates was never seen again by
anyone who knew him, and the lung and laborious
search instituted by the people of the locality
a .forded not a shred of evidence to hint at the
manner of his going off. Every dam and deserted
claim for miles around was carefully explored,
and every hollow or cranny into which his body
might have been washed by tire flood waters that
bailed down the gully was inspected in vain. Bates
had disappeared from the face.of the earth, and his
friends could not even form a rational conjecture
as to his fate. The great dam of the Star especi-
ally was carefully and thoroughly searched, under
the presumption that the missing man had re-
turned during the storm, and fallen into the deep
water whilst attempting to go along the bank,
which was the only convenient way across from
the mine to his house. The dam yielded no sign.
* # #
Dividends had fallen off painfully of late,
and the success of the new system of treating
pyrites suggested to the company the advisability
of sluicing the old sands. This bed of sand was
banked in the gully directly below the dam. A
large syphon was rigged over the embankment,
and a sluice carried the water and sand to the
boxes a little lower down. The syphon was fixed
up by an amateur in a hurry, and the joints were
not perfectly air-tight, so that she took air, and
ran down once or twice in a shift, when we had to
drive the bungs in, and give her a fresh start by
filling the pipes with water through a plug-hole in
the elbow. To do this, one man stood half-way
down the bank, and passed the buckets to his
mate on top, who fed the syphon, or “gave her a
drink,” as the process was termed.
One afternoon Bates and Naylor were on the
job, giving her a drink as usual, and expending an
unusual amount of profanity over the under-
taking. A cold, cutting wind blowing down the
gully increased the unpleasantness of the billet,
and intensified the bitterness of Sam’s language.
The young man had just filled the large iron
bucket, and was bringing it up to the hand of his
mate with a swing, when his foot slipped from
under him, and he fell face downwards, sprawling
on the wet clay ; the bucket, overturning
above, drenched him from top to toe, and he slid
down the bank and brought up with his feet in the
dam. As soon as he had recovered his breath, ho
was about to do credit to the occasion by an out-
burst of feeling, when his eyes became transfixed,
and his jaw fell, and, wet as it was, his hair stood
up on his head for horror. Staring out of the bank
directly before his face, and grinning into his very
teeth, was a man’s skull, clean and complete.
Sam glared at the apparition for a few seconds,
and slid further down, till he knelt shivering,
in the water. Then he pointed a trembling finger,
and gasped ;
“ Great Heaven, Naylor, there’s my o!e man 1 ”
That was “ the ole man,” or, at least, his mortal
remains. It is now presumed that Andrew Bates,
when the storm caught him, gave up his idea of
reaching the station, and returned towards his
home. Whilst crossing the gully along the bank
in the darkness, and contending with the wind, he
stepped off the track, not into the dam, but into a
narrow hole close to the bank that had once been
boxed in and served as a pump well for the
battery, but which was then timberless and nearly
full of slurry. The place was too narrow for a
man to help himself, once in it, and Bates must
have drowned or suffocated without a struggle.
The flood carried a quantity of mullock and
rubbish up out of the bed of the dam, completely
filling the old well, and hiding' the dead man
where mortal would never think of seeking him.
In passing up and down to water the syphon,
we had worn steps in the steep bank, and the top
of the skull had been exposed for some time, and
formed a good footing without exciting any atten-
tion whatever, till Sam’s foot, in slipping, broke
away a big cake of clay, and exposed the whole of
the ghastly face to the light of day.
We dug the bank down, and found the skeleton
standing, erect and entire.
“Lord forgive me! for five months I’ve been
stepping on my ole man’s head ! ” said Sam.

Women’s Suffrage.
Deau Bulletin, -In your issue of 23rd July
your correspondent, “ Charles dunor,’’ advances
lIn; following projositious to prove that women
should still rank with lunatics, aboriginals, con-
victs, and the hke so far as political power is con-
cerned ; —(1) “ The desirableness of women’s
suffrage is a matter readily disposed of.” (2)
“ Women’s tendency is to vote directly against
her male relative’s interest and support unre-
servedly any smug mediocrity who travels solely
on liquor abolition, Bible-in-schools,’ 1 &c. (3)
The writer can't see why a woman shouldn’t bo
content to let “ her nearest male relative” vote
for her. (4) Women are better as they are,
because the male elector takes more care of them
in matters of legislation than they could of them-
selves. These are the heads of Mr. Junor’s dis-
course, slightly condensed, and they are brutal,
cold-blooded Toryism of the narrowest order.
About a hundred years ago your correspondent,
if he had been alive then, wouldn't have had a
vote for exactly the same reasons. In those days
(I) the desirability of any man except a big land-
lord having a vote, was “ easily disposed of ; ” (2)
the poor man couldn’t be entrusted with a vote,
because he exhibited a tendency to us it against
the big landlord’s interest, and because he was so
ignorant that he would support unreservedly
any smug mediocrity, &c. ; (3) the writer of
those days didn't sec why the ordinary citizen,
like Mr. Junor, shouldn't let his landlord vote
for him; and (4) ho was bettor as he was,
because his landlord took more care of him than
lie could of himself. The democracy objected to
all this ancient rot, however, on the ground that
they didn’t want to bo taken care of, and that
every sane human being had a right to help in
g ivtrning himself, and if lie did it badly it was
Ids own look-out. After long years of conflict the
masses have, to a very large extent, carried their
point, and having secured a great measure of
liberty for themselves they straightway demand
that no one else shall have any. And to support
this contention they use exactly the same
arguments which the} 7 denounced so violently
when they were used against themselves. The
old Tory said that the workman wasn’t fit
to have the franchise because he was a kind of in-
ferior animal, and that he shouldn’t have it
because he manifested a tendency to vote against
his (the said blatant Tory’s) interests; the modern
democrat says that women are not fit to have the
franchise, because they are inferior animals, and
that if they had it they would vote against his
(the equally blatant democrat’s) interests. Neither
of them cares a straw for anybody’s interests
except his own ; they are equally democratic—in
looking after their own interests, and equally con-
servative in their utter neglect of the interests of
everybody else.
# # #
The plea that no woman should have the suffrage
because her sex lias a blind and incurable tendency
to support the solemn snuffler who wants to
abolish liquor, or to have the Bible read in schools,
and because she would vote solidly for the person
with the beautiful moustache; and, further,
because she is a priest-governed individual who
follows unquestioningly the instructions of her
spiritual advisers, is interesting, no doubt. But
the accusation comes with a bad grace from the
male alleged democracy which has for so many
years supported such dull mediocrities as Play-
ford, Griffith, MTlwraith, Gillies, Munro, More-
head. Vogel, MCulloch, and their kind ; which
voted for Parkes through 30 solid .years because of
his hair, and supported Jawbone Neild by reason
of his moustache, which is so miserably priest-
ridden that it sacrifices its interests and its
country, for the sake of Dutch William and his
servant Abigail, which votes for Freetrade because
the big importers tell it to do so, and sells itself
to the Single-tax faddists, and howls itself hoarse
over teetotalism and the Bible-in-schools, and
which never in all its imbecile career once knew
when to come in out of the wet. The spectacle of
the male elector denying the suffrage to women
because they might do what he does himself, and
has always done, and seemingly will do till the
end of time, is pitiable hypocrisy. If it is right
for man to refuse the franchise to woman lest her
enfranchisement should in some way injure his
interests, then it is right for the rich man to refuse
the franchise to the poor man for exactly the same
reasons. Each one is looking after his own interests
and doesn’t care a straw for anything beyond.
Further, I notice that another correspondent
advances, as an unanswerable settlement of the
suffrage question, the statement —possibly quite a
true one—that “except child-bearing there is
nothing women can do that cannot bo better done
by men, including cooking, nursing, and macrame
work.” This idea is capable of indefinite exten-
sion. There is nothing the pauper can do that the
man who isn’t a pauper can’t do better, therefore
the pauper shouldn’t have a vote. There is noth-
ing the Australian can do up to date—barring
athletics—that the Englishman or the American
can’t do better, therefore the Australian shouldn’t
have a vote. There is hardly anything the nigger
can do that the white man can’t do better, there-
fore the nigger should be squashed. And by the
same rule no man should have a vote unless there
is something that he can do better than anybody
else on earth, and the franchise should be restricted
to about a hundred individuals, all told. - Yours,
&c., Titus Salt.
WHAT
IT FEELS
LIKE
HAVE you ever observed how a man behaves
when he has the toothache? Life is full
of bitterness to him, and swear words linger on
his lips. He arises after a night of torment,
comes down to breakfast in a temper the reverse
of angelic, and, having bestowed a vicious kick
upon the harmless, necessary cat which presumed
to meet him on the stairs, seats himself at the
table, and assumes a scowling expression
eminently calculated to sour the contents of the
cream jug. Asked whether his toothache is not a
little easier, he Snaps out, “You don’t suppose
it will stop aching just because you think it ought
to.” Ho gulps down a few strong expressions,
finds fault with the chop, the coffee, the behaviour
of the children, and gives particular prominence
to the fact that very few men are blessed with a
long-suffering disposition like his. Watch him as
he walks to business in the city. How affection-
ately he presses his hand to the jaw from whence
springs all the cause of his woe and mentally
exclaims, “Oh, that mine enemy had this torture.”
The world has ceased to have any charm for him.
He even forgets how to be entertaining in the
presence of his favourite barmaid, and manifests
displeasure when she smiles at the figure he cuts
while trying to hold a sip of brandy in that corner
of his mouth where the affected molar is located.
This exquisite torture must have been the lot of
Shakespere, when he explained the truism,
“ For there was never yet philosopher
That could endure the toothache patiently.”
Had Shakespere lived in the present age, how-
ever, he would have known that there is no need
to endure, either partially or otherwise, the
torture of a decaying tooth, since toothache may
be effectually and speedily conquered by the use
of St. Jacob’s Oil. There is, in fact, no bodilv
pain that it will not remove. It penetrates gently
and quickly ; it soothes perfectly ; it heals surely ;
it cures promptly, permanently and without re-
action. Thus invaluable medicine has taken the
leading prizes wherever exhibited, and has firmly
established itself as the best domestic remedy in
the world for rheumatism, neuralgia, backache,
and a host of minor complaints which are the
daily cause of so much misery and suffering;

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Queensland and N.S. Wales did not play their
ver.i best men in Inst 1 Saturday’s interprovincial football-
match. Three Brisbane cracks -Brcreton, Beale, and
Beattie— have broken collar-bones, and so has Abbott, the
Sydney ’Varsity champion. Nice, healthy game, football.
Tot. Mutineer, the most sensational Sydney player, was
overlooked by the selection committee, Mr. M. Arnold.
Toe crowd won’t stand this again.
* * #
Proceedings nearly started with a free fight in
fie grand-stand. A wretch wfiio had drink in him asked
a man not to intercept the view —asked him by placing a
lighted cigar on the nape of the other man’s neck. The
r .7w which arose out of this event exhi'arated tiie assem-
blage, who greeted the teams with a storm of welcome.
» # *
The Bananalanders wore dark-blue jerseys and
white pants. The home team donned white pants and
awful jerseys-voluminous sickly blue-bags, apparently
all made for the Oft. Tin. stature of Monty Arnold (line
umpire), tor the slack of those bags hung about the
jj.y. Males men in folds, and streamed out in the
breeze, and served in place of back-hair or tails
for the other fellows to drag them down by.
The game started with the wind behind Brisbane, War-
brick having won the toss from Colquhoun. In the first
ten minutes the Brisbaneites got three free kicks for off-
side, and Graham just missed scoring a goal off the third,
fir the hall struck the goal-post and bounded back into
p'ay. The game became a series of scrums—just human
dog-lights—heads bobbing, bodies surging, legs and feet
flying at all angles for the ball amid thwacks, grants, and
thuds. Every time the Brisbane forwards came out on
top, either by bearing their opponents down and dribbling
the'ball through their midst, or by “heeling” it out and
“feeding” their halves, who would dash away with it
evidently intending to get over the Sydney line alive or
dead. “Jimmy” M’Mahon, the Sydney full-back, is a
player who uses his head. He needed to. When-
ever a Brisbaneite came whirring through the
nick the vast crowd would climb on the fence
and enjoy itself, and yell, “ Holy smoke, Jimmy !” “ Flop
h in, Jimmy!” “Oh, Sydney! my native land!” “Flop
biin !” M‘Mahon always charged the enemy bull-fashion,
clumping his head into the other fellow’s stomach and
tossing him high in the air. Twice in the first half, by
v-,and rushes which completely paralysed the home team,
the Brisbaneites got over. The whole Brisbane team
used to gel under way simultaneously and come full
steam ahead ; they were too heavy and clever for the
enemy, and, M’Mahon exoepted, the Sydney backs were
palpably weak on the defensive. The second try gave the
crowd a rare sensation. Maori Warbrick, the Brisbane
captain, got the ball and came along at a great
p-ioe. One Sydneyite tried to collar him low, but
Warbrick smote him in the eye with ins knee-
joint and sent him yards. Then two more Syd-
neyites rushed at Warbrick together, and the
three strong athletes collided with a crash, and fell in a
heap. Next instant Queensland scored a try, and then
everybody saw that Warbrick was laid out. He was like
a man that had been stepped on by a thunderbolt. Half-
time was called, and as no barrow was handy to remove
the dead, he was carried in. He resurrected and came
out again in the second half as game as ever. During the
interval the crowd’s ardour was not suffered to cool, for a
handsome procession of assorted athletes in silken gar-
ments of every hue, jigged in stately order around the
arena. This was the 1090 yards handicap. It was a
gorgeous prance, and ended in a splendid race between
Flett and Dalryinple—the latter winning.
* * *
The second half of the match was a repet’tion of
the first—just scrum, scrum, serum, more choking, more
grassing, more travail, more gruel, more Queensland than
N.S.W. always. Now and then a Sydney man would make
a brilliant individual effort, but be was always garrotted
before he got dangerous. Sydney never, scored a point.
The feature of the match was the magnificent play of
O'Shea, the Brisbane half back. ID secured three tries
out of four. Towards the close of the day’s play, he made
a wonderful ran right from his own 25. lie is a light-
weight—a flyer. He darted in and out through the
opposing team like a swallow, and when he sped over the
line and scored fourth try for Queensland, 8010 lungs in
full blast cheered him to the echo. The 21st interpro-
vincial match was a real good time for Queensland.
“Tatters ill” is playing the game a little low
down in having a l>ig sweep on all the minor races about
Sydney, and, as a matter of course, ho now never raises
the full nuinh 'r, and lias to draw pro-rala. Another
trouble is that Taft. I is horse-owners know who draw
their nags, thus giving the shady owners—and unfortu-
nately the majoiily belong to the list -the opportunity of
blackmailing the drawer of a horse under the threat of
scratching him. Tattersall had hetle' ‘go slow” or he
will kill the goose.
# * #
A Queensland correspondent suggests to The
Bulletin’ that. Tatt. should write under registered cover
to the drawers of all unclaimed prizes in Its sweep during
the last five years. Sliou’d this not lead to the amounts
being claimed, our correspondent urges that the money
should hi distributed among the charities—it is the
people’s in mey, and Taltersall’s profits are certainly good
enough without retaining any unclaiiu.il surplus.
# # *
How will Go Idard fare against Jackson ? Well,
despite that draw, very badly. Firstly, he is too slow ;
next, he is a “rasher,” and rushing suits the black’s
style, as impact adds force to his blow. Jackson may be
beaten by a in ui equally quick and clever, witli greater
reach and height, or a panther of the S iliivan type may
break down his guard, or a small *r man like Mitchell may
wear him <1 >wn in a ring tight, as Siyers and Mace did to
their bigger opponents, but one who is, though slow, a
ru slier and nothing more, cannot hope tel accomplish the
trick.
# * *
Charley Dunn, recently defeated by Greedon, is,
ib’spite his enormous strength, not cutout fora pugilist,
lie is too heavy for a middleweight, and has not reach or
height enough to meet a goal big man. In this respect
lie is somewhat like .L in Smith. D spite his nuggety
build gu | weight Ditt.ii is very.active, ami lias shown good
foim as a sprinter.
# # #
A well-known trotting-horse owner, when
twitted about the “ crank ” state of the sport in Sydney,
remarked that the present style of handicapping is the
whole cause of the trouble. Be that as it may, trotting-
-111-ii have rsi'y themselves to blame that Canterbury,
Riseliill, Moorefield and Warwick Farm, do not place
valuable trotting fixtures on their programmes.
# # #
How little we know of the sport in Australia
may b ; judged from tli ■ fact that whilst we cannot raise
a 2.30 pei foinicr, no less than 459 horses have trotted in
America in Hums between 2.8| and 2.20. Belle Hamlin
amt Globe, in May last, at Philadelphia, U.S., trotted
jheir mile in 2.131, the fastest lime done in double
harness.
# # *
Hear Bulletin, —The axemen of Tasmania are
going to have a big chopping match at Latiche iu Novem-

Donizetti, by Marvellous, has just been carry-

* # #
Scobie, of Ballarat, scorns to have a couple of
heart-breaking animals in Bolton and llavilah. The
former won’t stand spurs when an extra effort is required
of him, and the latter must win all the way if he’s to win
at all. Last Saturday week, at Flemington, Havilah
looked like pulling ojR the open Hurdle Race until Kim-
berley lushed up to him, whereupon Scobie’s horse
declined to consider the matter any further.
sf # #
Grand Duke Alexis of Russia telegraphed to
the Epsom stewards to postpone the Derby for an hour
because he had missed his train. However, this was t o
large an order for even a body of “Turf Tories’’(and
snobbery is very rife in the racing world) to execute, so
the Grand Duke arrived just in time to find that he had
backed a loser. The incident is interesting. It shows
how the effusive “ loyalty ” of the Londoners who go wild
over anything with a big foreign handle to its name had
persuaded Alexis that the Derby was being specially got
up for his entertainment.
#
Absurdity for a white man to fight a thick-
skulled nigger. How can you give a nigger a black eye,
or a swollen lip, or a flatter nose ?
* # #
Three years ago Redleap was the deadest of
“ dead birds” for the Flcmington G.N. Steeplechase, but
the gelding hurt his foot after winning the Hurdle Race
and had to he scratched. Eaglet, however, proved good
enough to complete Miller’s “double,” and so the
stable got there all the same without exposing the fact
that “ big ” jumping Was Redleap’s/orfe. In 18S9 as in
1892 the Steeplechase was the event that Dante’s son
had been specially prepared for. The Hurdle was
his preliminary canter, so to speak. Perhaps he
was a trifle faster at four years of age than
he is now, but, anyway, a horse that could
run clean away from Corythus (who subsequently
put up a record for a mile on the flat) must be a
phenomenon amongst jumpers of both classes when
thoroughly well. And the Millers, having got then-
horse well, only wanted to see him win the Hurdle ra -e
again to feel confident that they held a mortgage on the
Steeple which was to have been Redleap’s second bite in
’B9. How patient these rich people can afford to be !
# * #
A Singleton (N.S.W.) chemist the other day
gave three boxes of pills as a prize at a local sports
meeting.
# * #
The Charters Towers miners to a man backed
Paddy Slavia in his recent encounter, Slavin having
worked a number of years on the field.
# * *
At every race-meeting held in a big Northern
township, there is a sweep promoted on the principal
event: this year it was worth £1350. The winning
number was held by two nymphs da pave, each one
netting £675. At latest accounts the two sirens had
started a barouche, etc.
# # #
The other day, the well-known cyclist, Ted
Elliott, died at Carlton, of typhoid. Over 150 cyclists in
uniform attended the funeral. At Adelaide, in 18S9,
Elliott put up the world’s record for half a mile.
# * »
In half-crown sweeps, got up among friends, a
Sydney barber drew Redleap three times running, out of
a hat. Then he concluded lie had the “straight griffin”
from some benign angel. He backed Redleap heavily,
and made a pile. This same barber did a similar thing
when Assyrian won the Melbourne Cup, and so obtained
the needful to set him up in his present business.

■‘Augur” Chapman meant to leave an Aus-
tralian turf-history, which is now open for anyone else to
undertake.
# # #
The Victorian anti-totalisator party aver that
the machine is not a success in S.A., and was previously
abolished after being tried. The fact is, the totalisator
was formerly used at every meeting, and was done to
death, but now it is restricted to only first-class meetings,
ami has proved a distinct success.
* * #
“ Yorkshireman,” of Sydney, and “ Yorkshire-
man,” of Newcastle, sporting tipster and bookmaker
respectively, died within a few days of one another.
Bookmaker—tipster, one cannot live without the other.
# # *
“ To show what little hurt even the most serious
sparring does trained men, 1 will mention that Slavin, in
less than five minutes after being knocked out, was walk-
ing upstairs alone and unassisted to his dressing-room,
and talking to his friends. Perhaps the boobies who ask
questions in Parliament will make a note of this little
fact. It may please them to know also that the arrange-
ments at the club were perfect—or would bo if provision
were made for speedy exit in case of fire or other alarm.”
—Reeekee.
# * #
It is strange that none of the placed horses in the
Flemington Grand National Steeplechase (Redleap, Confi-
dence, and Wellington) had ever been over the big jumps
on that course before ; while horses like Jack’s the Lad,
Freeman, Shanks, Egyptian, and Blister, who had all
been jumped there previously, fell at different periods of
the race.
# # *
Tim Griffo-Barron fight ended in a draw, much
to the disgust of a large audience, after 21 rounds. Griffo,
weighing 9st. lib., and giving away 121b5., was very much
above himself, otherwise he would probably have won.
This young man w ill probably be a physical wreck within
a year or two. The other man couldn't hit him he got
heavily home on the Rocks champiqp's nose once —and
Griffo had him dazed once or twice but lacked strength to
follow up and finish him.
What is Arbitration ? An agreement not
to agree to any decision that is not agreeable to
either one of the agreeing parties.

Statutory Declaration.
ITHOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 515 George Street,

solemnly and sincerely declare as follows :
Whereas, I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried several
Doctors and many Remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas,
I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rassmussen’s
PURELY HERBAL “UNIVERSAL ALFALINE
PILLS,” which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare
COMPLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and
although this is over five years ago, I have remained well ever
since.
' THE ALF ALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration, conscientiously believing the same to
be true.
THOS. MUDIMAN.

November, 1891, before me,
S. T. Whiddon, Justice of the Peace.

A Walgett (N.S.W.) scribe :
Then (o the well-appreciated and inspiring: strains of
Miss O’Hara’s touch, that exemplar of altitudinal har :
niony, which is called the “poetry of motion” in the
hackneyed vernacular of the fashionable aisthetic world,
made, for once in a way, all nature within the four walls
of the old Oddfellows’ Hall more palpably akin than it is
usually accredited to be ly those who never surrender
themselves to the dulcet influence of the melodious
Terpsichore.
Our colonial! There’s langwidge ! There’s melli-
fluous flow of long - backed pa ter ! There’s
imagery ! There’s rybuck ! The analytical
and extr tspective critic, prostrate with over-
powered and captive admiration of the scintil-
lating penumbra of effulgence ingeminated in
the alpine eloquence that shoots meteorlike
from Walgett to the Algolian Hesperides, and
limelights a throbbing universe into palpitating
happiness, will apprehend, with one eagle-like,
penetrative glance of the orbicular vision of that
mysterious incognito that the vulgar vocabulary
designates the mind, that here is the one hitherto
unattainable opportunity for Alf. Bennett to con-
summate the refrigerated yearnings of a pulsating
lifetime, and secure for the typographical dis-
sertations of the Sydney Evening News the
iipheres-harmonising crowquill of a master essayist,
who can caligraph up to the altitudinous plane of
the ambitions intelligence of that monurnal
journal's erudite subscribers. Yap !

The Fact of the Matter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m wonderin’ why those? fellers who go huildin’
chipper ditties,
’Bout the rosy times out drovin’, an’ the dust an’
death of cities,
Don’t sling the bloomin’ office, strike some drover
for a billet,
And soak up a.l the glory that comes handy while
they fill it.
P’r’aps it’s fun to travel cattle or to picnic with
merinos,
But the drover don't catch on, sir, not much high-
class rapture he knows.
As for sleepin’ on the plains there in the shadder
of the spear-grass,
That's liked best by the Juggins with a spring-bed
an’ a pier-glass.
An’ the camp fire, an’ the freedom, and the blanky
constellations,
The ’possum-rug an’ billy, an’ the togs an’ stale
ole rations—
It’s strange they’re only raved about by coves that
dress up pretty,
An’ sport a wife, an’ live on slap-up tucker in the
city.
I've tickled beef in my time clear from Clarke to
Riverina,
An’ shifted sheep all round the shop, but blow
me if I've seen a
Single blanky hand who didn’t buck at pleasures
of this kidney,
And wouldn’t trade his blisses for a flutter down
in Sydney.
Night-watches are delightful when the stars are
really splendid
To the chap who’s fresh upon the job, but, you
bet, his rapture's ended
When the rain comes down in sluice-heads, or the
cuttin’ hailstones palter,
An’ the sheep drift off before the wind, an’ the
horses strike for shelter.
Don’t take me for a howler, but I find it come
annoyin’
To hear these fellers rave about the pleasures we’re
enjoyin’,
When p’r’aps we’ve nothin’ better than some fluky
water handy,
An’ they’re right on all the lickers—rum, an’
plenty beer an’ brandy.
The town ig dusty, may be, but it isn’t worth the
curses
’Side the dust a feller swallers an’ the blinded
thirst he nurses
When he’s on the hard macadam, where the jum-
bueks cannot browse, an’
The wind is in his whiskers, an’ he follers twenty
thousan’.
This drovin’on the plain, too, it’s all O.K. when
the weather
Isn’t hot enough to curl the soles right off your
upper leather,
Or so cold that when the mornin’ wind comes hissin’
through the grasses
You can feel it cut your eyelids like a whip-lash
as it passes.
Then there’s bull-ants in the blankets, an’ a lame
horse, an’ muskeeters.
An’ a D.T. boss like Halligan, or one like Humpy
Peters,
Who is mean about the tucker, an’ can curse from
start to sundown,
An’ can fight like fifty devils, an’ whose growler’s
never run down.
Yes, I wonder why the fellers what go buildin’
chipper ditties
’Bout the rosy times out drovin’ an’ th’ dust an’
death of cities,
Don’t sling the bloomin’ office, strike ole Peters
for a billet,
An’ soak up all the glory that comes handy while
they fill it.
E. DYSON.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The Garibaldi mine at Hillgrove is worked Dr
gold and antimony. It has had a struggling existence
for years. Fred. Clissold, lately deceased, dropped his
monev in it. The present owners appear lo be buoyed up
hy a statement made by the late N.S.W. Government
geologist—“ That as depth is attained, the antimony will
lessen and the gold increase.” With all respect to Mr.
Wilkinson’s judgment, this is hy no means the rule, and
one need not go beyond Hillgrove itself lo see it. Hill-
grove is bounded by two great gorges, 1600 ft. deep, and
the Garibaldi mine is on the tableland between them.
There is comparatively little antimony in Hie Baker s
Creek or*Sunlight lodes, which are in one gorge, hut in
the other, the lodes contain as great a percentage of anti-
mony at the bottom of the gorge as on the surface.
# # #
The S.M. Herald “ Mining Notes ” are, at any
rate, as a rule, written with care, and Hie writer, in (he
absence of judgment, seldom praises or condemns any,-
thing, consequently the appearance of the statement that
(here are thousands of tons of gold-bearing ore exposed in
Hie Pamhula tunnel mine awaiting some efficient method
of treating fine gold, is a slaggerer. It is what one might
expect in a manager’s report, from which possibly it lias
been taken. “Awaiting a more economical mode of
treatment” is an old familiar “ wheeze.” What is wanted
in many cases is a process which will reduce rocks to the
fineness of flour, separate every particle of visible or
invisible gold, collect every grain of pyrites, ana take the
gold therefrom, and do it all for a few shillings a ton ; or,
better still, make up any deficiency of gold in the ore.
# # *
It is reported that in washing away some 200
loads from the tail-race over 12ozs. of gold were got from
the Spa sluicing claim. The return seems Lo be too good
to lie true. Anyhow, it is very unreasonable to imagine
that old John Wallace, with all his sluicing experience,
would tackle an expenditure of over £30,000 before getting-
an ounce of gold unless he knew’ what he was going for.
# * *
Occasionally a stray newspaper paragraph
informs the public that the Homeward Bound claim, at
Yalwal, N.S.W., continues to give good yields of gold, but
the real figures are never submitted. Why don’t the
Government, as owners, compel lessees to give publicity
to their yields? This mine was profitably worked for
many years by the former owners, who are said to have
raised £30,000 worth of gold, but, as their returns were
never published, few knew of its existence, and fewer of
its location. It was then purchased for £19,000 cash by
the present ow’ners, w ho have passed through much tribu-
lation, mads up of law-suits and low-grade ore. It seems
to he in gold what White Rock is iu silver-a network of
irregularly-defined veins, which cannot he raised without
quarrying the entire body.
* * *
Big things are predicted for Bendigo in the near
future. The cool-headed Bendigo miner, if he speaks the
truth, w ill admit that these sudden spurts occasioned hy
wonderfully-improved yields have occurred periodically
for the last 20 years. As a rule, every month two or
three mines, either hy the opening up of a new lift or the
extension of a crosscut into new country strike fresh
makes of gold, and when it happens that half-a-dnzen
minees do this simultaneously instead of one after the
0 her, then theie is a temporary boom sometimes lasting
half a year, during which new spapers make asses of them-
selves and fool the public. The only feature about the
Bendigo boom that distinguishes it from others, is the
fact that two well-known lines of reef have been con-
siderably extended.
« • »
The plant of the Ironclad mine, Cargo, N.8.W.;
has been sacrificed. It was worked by an English Co.,
and who worked the company Heaven only knows. After
spending many thousands in machinery, including a
chlorination plant, and other thousands in development,
the mine has been abandoned. The goal is largely
associated with copper. At times the show was run at a
profit, it was said, hut no figures are available.

The “ Turf Attorney-General “ Yorkshireman ”—w ho

made of carefully-selected ■ stone,' and in his. wild
enthusiasm dropped much m we.v. He sent three prac-
tical men to inspect whose warnings lie refused to accept.
It is reported that something worth working has at last
been found on the mine.

# * *
Whenever the Victorian Government diamond-
drills discover anything of any consequence the dist rict is
invariably in the hands of a fat syndicate which is never
heard of till the numbers are up. Remarkable also that
the Melbourne papers never howl about any particular
field for investment till the whole country-side is grabbed
hy the large speculators, and they are offering the chances
to a hungry public at an “ enormous sacrifice.”
• * *
Some Chinese, working for a European gold-
mine owner, at Port Darwin, N.T., came across a golden
leader, said to run lib. weight of gold to each bucket of
stone. They held a chin-chin, carefully covered up all
traces of work, and interviewed their employer as to
securing a tribute of part of his claim. But the Aladdin’s-
cave yarn had been whispered by every wind, and the
employer refused. Again and again Hie Chinkies tried to
drive a bargain ; again and again were they repelled.
When at last they saw the boss was adamant, they retired
for a final confab. Then, their spokesman returned and
said—“ You lefuse offec tliboot; all li. You welly ole
man; I young man. You die soon ; I gettee gole all li.
You no can find ’im. I wait: by Vhy, you die ; I gettee
all li. So long!” And the European has not found the
gorgeous pocket, though he has spent months in searching
for it.
