The trust-moneys collected for the widows of
(he Lithgow miners, killed a few years back, were, it
appears, “invested” in Abigail’s hank 1
Probably the roughest thing that ever happened
to Francis Abigail (now awaiting trial in connection with
Hi° Australian Banking Co. troubles) was the documents 1
which were found in the pockets of M'Namara (who is
also awaiting tiiai) when the latter person was arrested.
One of these purported to be a copy of a letter written
hy M'Namara to Abigail, about 15 months ago, in which
that gentleman addresses his boss as follows: “Perhaps ,
it would not be out of place to mention just now the fact
that £35,431 15s. was taken out of the hank’s cotters hy
former directors, and £17,736 15s. 2d. by the presentbcaid.
I will now leave you to judge for yourself, at the same
time answer your own question. I have before me at this I
moment the particulars of these amounts, consequently I I
cannot refrain from stating that the details are sufficiently I
sickening to make even the most hardened ruffian blush. I (
may also mention at this stage that Mr. Bull is not as
black as he is painted. If he is, well, when he sees Hie
matter now before me lie will certainly turn pure
w hile. My adv ice to you at the present is this;
1 Don’t allow the drama to become too ludicrous ,
before my return to the bank.’ ” Also, there are sundry
hideously ungrammatical letters from Abigail, in one of
which he observes that when Hie Bill passes authorising
the ancient pawnshop to become a hauls, of issue, and
more capital is obtained, *‘a complete change will have s
to be made in the arrangements ; ” and no less than three |
certificates signed by Abigail attesting that the securities (
are perfectly correct, and “largely in excess of Hie value j
of the amounts obtained against them.” It was after
these documents were signed that M'Namara declared the
details of the management were “ sufficiently sickening
to make the most hardened ruffian blush.” Apparently ‘
the Australian Banking Co. was not a happy, family. |
According to the evidence, one of the directors was last ■
heard of sleeping in a sixpenny bed at the Model Lodging
House. He ought to be an interesting man if he could be 1
found, and it is lo lie hoped the liquidators will have a
look round the Domain for him.

By section 11, sub-section 11, of the Trades
Disputes Conciliation and Arbitration Act, the Bioken
Hill strikers have power to lodge aaapplication with Hie
Clerk of Awards requesting that the dispute be referred
to a council of conciliation. The other side refusing to
join then the council of conciliation have power to
report that they have been unable to secure a settlement of
the dispute. That stage reached, the miners may request
the Clerk of Awards (sec. 12) to refer the matter to the
council of Arbitration. Of course, nothing will be done,
but tills procedure will show up the weak spots in the
capitalistic refusal to come to an agreement.

Graham Berry and itis fellow (Melb. Mercantile
Bank) liquidation-investigators assert already that of over
£1,000,000 sterling lent, at least £835,000 comprises bad
or doubtful debts.
# # *
Some months ago a leading Maori land
coal mining company gave notice of a reduction of
wages. The men kicked and formed a union, and
the notice was withdrawn. Shortly afterwards a sudden
increase of trade necessitated the starting of another shift
and men were engaged for that purpose. Eight or nine
weeks afterwards the trade as suddenly decreased, and 113
men were discharged—nearly all of them being unionists.

It is on record that the authorities of the
Sydney building society to which Mr. Fogarty, now of
South America, vvasdately attached, were careless or coin-
passionate enough to allow that gentleman leg-bail
instead of locking him up as soon as he confessed. Have
Hie society’s accounts yet been dissected hy a cold-
blooded, cynical accountant ?
* * *
A few weeks ago. the Commercial Agency,
Trading and Banking (Jo., of Sydney, went on a reef, and
—under the new Act for the relief of sinking hanks—got
three years to settle up. The terms were exceptionally
favourable for the hank, inasmuch as only 40 per cent, of
the liabilities were to be paid in the first two years, the
remaining 60 per cent, coming due in the third year.
Now a large ticket in Hie bank window announces that
fixed deposits are received at usual rates, and that
interest is allowed on current accounts, &c. There-
fore it would he interesting to know if the court
which sanctioned the arrangement with the old
depositors can make any effectual provision whereby
the old deposits and the new ones will be kept separate,
for it they both go into one common account, and if the
hank should, by any chance, go aground again—say in the
third year—then the new deposits must, hy Hie ordinary
process of book-keeping, go in paying off the old ones.
If there isn’t enough money to go round, the man whose
deposit falls due first has the best show, and the person
who puts in his cash now, for three years, obviously doesn’t
get a chance till almost all the old liabilities are paid off—
these same liabilities which the company couldn’t,meet
when they fell dive a little while ago. Further, it would
he interesting lo know if the framers of the-Act ever
contemplated that a company-which h-.d to get an exten-
sion of time upon its old deposits should at once start to
take in new ones; and if there is any definite provision in
Hie Act whereby Hie new depositor shall he informed,
before lie puts his money in, of the fact that the old ones
aren’t paid.

Thomas Price, manager.” It isn’t every man who has
the gall to sign Ida name to his own biography in this
fashion. Perhaps—happy thought!—it isn’t every mining
manager who feels that his reputation will hear such a
strain.
# * *
Ballarat wants the Government to drain Sebas-
topol plateau, and Bendigo has an axe to grind. The
shafts of Lansell’s ISO mine, the Pandora, and Hustler’s
have each reached 2000 feet and over in depth, and are in
a direct line Fast and West on the three main lines of
reef. Bendigo men think the Government ought to
undertake driving cross-cuts from one shaft to another at
a good depth, when it is certain innumerable unknown
reefs would be intersected. The Bendigo field is a net-
work of reefs, though only three main lines are worked.
# # *
The recent sale of the Kentish mine was owing
to the trustees of Millionaire .I. H. Watson’s estate refus-
ing to pay the money required for prospecting work there.
The price realised by selling to the adjoining Co., the
Carlisle, £3500, is just about the value of the
machinery on the ground. The bulk of Watson s fortune
came from the Iventish, out of which he took 24 tons of
gold.
• * •
The Raub Australasian syndicate up in the
Malay Peninsula has never had any luck ; all the wild
rumours of wealth beyond the wildest dream of avarice
have ended in nothing hut calls.
# # *
One man up North managed to get level with
the Mount Morgan gang. He had bought shares fairly
cheap, and when they stood at £l4 he started to growl
about them in the presence of some of Hie biggest share-
holders and some talkative outsiders one night in a
champagney atmosphere. P.y dint of incessant nagging
he huffed one or two of the crowd, one of whom, a, very
big man indeed, at, last asked him if he would lake £l4
for his shares. “Pooh!” said the holder, “£lll as 4
you or any of your crowd would dare to give £l4 ! The
big man, in a Vomer, said it- was « Ir-egahi, - whereupon
the holder instantly got his signature to a contract-note.
The lucky seller made £45,000 profit by the deal, and has
since, until the other day, been enjoying himself
thoroughly in Ireland.
# * *
Members of the Mercer tribe, Matthew Davies’
wife’s people, are drawing salaries for liquidating some of
the burst-up Davies “companies.” There’s a deal of the
“ select family party ” about this arrangement.
it # *
Taking into consideration the way the unions
have been drained of funds l>y strikes, the money rolls in
famously for the Broken Hill miners. Most of w hom, by
the way, have resolved to exhaust their private resources
ere tapping Hie funds. The strike may last six months.
* * *
As in the maritime strike, the mine-owners will
meet the men in conference if the latter will first give
up their agreement and everything else they arc fighting
for. Which means, that there will be nothing left to
confer about.
# # •
The Broken Hill mine-owners got a month’s
suspension of their lease conditions on the ground that
they were prevented working their mines hy pickets of
the strikers. As they had not a “ free ” labourer on the
ground this excuse was pretty thin.
* * *
There is talk of challenging the authorities of
one Austialiau assurance-office —the challenge to take the
form of a criminal prosecution for issuing false balance-
sheets. Experts, who should be capable of giving an un-
biassed opinion, declare the position of the office in
question to be ludicrous and pitiable. This is the
“ straight-griffin ” to some rather influential personages.
To Readers of “The Bulletin.”
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to Tub
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, w ho
gets the guileless public to w ork for him for noth-
ing-. Short stories, orballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t \vrite a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.

Tales of the Riverine.
IN PUGGA MILLY REACH,
(F OR THE BULLETIN.)
Big, burly Tom M'Grundy, with the thirst
of a sponge, features of a red, red rose, the heart of
an angel, and the financial genius of a Wilkins
Micawber—his I.O.XJ.’s and P.N.’s would have
covered the Old Man Plain with a pavement of
tesselatcd indebtedness—drove up to the boats as
they were raising steam for the trip to Echuca.
They had dropped down from the wharves over-
night and had tied up at the south bank of the
Murrumbidgee, just below the bend in the stream
where the 150-acre police paddock runs into
Mungadel station. Both the steamers—the Jessie
Jane and the Resolution —were under M‘Grundy’s
agency, and while there was no dodge that Mac.
could not work to steal a march upon an opposing
firm—the ring yet with the way he “did”
M'Cullooh’s Hay manager (Fehon, present
N.S.W. Railway Commissioner, was boss of the
big concern then) out of ,£2OOO worth of wool
freight- he was studiously just to the several
owners he represented. He tapped them for loans
and drinks with equal impartiality, and he gave
the “little” men with the one cranky steamer
and a solitary crazy barge the same show for
Cutting a plummy slice out of the rich cake of the
season’s wool-traffic as he did the bigger man who
Had half-a-dozen steamers and twice as many
barges. No flunkeyish, kiss-his-hand favouritism
to the well-in owner was ever shown by Tom
M'Grundy, and it would have been better for the
small men on the rivers had all the agents and
managers followed his lead.
The Jessie Jane was a small man’s craft
skippered by its owner and under the double dis-
advantages of carrying with its cargo an
eternally-on-the-eve-of-going-to-blazes boiler and
a tremendous mortgage; while the Resolution
belonged to a man who was being helped literally
by a bank, and who, though bound to go under
some time like all bank-made creatures—some
day, I’ll x-ecite certain legends of a Riverina bank-
sweating room—would be “on top” for five
seasons or so. Therefore, Tom M‘Grundy would,
without doubt, have found it in the long run
much more to his own profit had he favoured the
Resolution. But that was not his way of doing
business.
As he drew rein on the sandy hummock he
hailed the craft which the freshet was jostling
restlessly against one another. The gilded, brassy
Resolution, with barge lashed a-beam, lay astern i
of the beggarly Jessie Jane that looked as if the 1
current half-year’s interest on the mortgage now ]
just due would sink her to the bottom of the i
river; and the big boat now and then stuck her
nose more viciously into her shabby rival as 1
though she would smash her from very contempt. '
The Resolution and her barge were carrying
twelve hundred bales of “way back” clips, and i
the tarpaulined pile towered majestically over the 1
four hundred, which were all the Jessie and her ]
barge were permitted by stern Tummy Freeman, ]
the Echuca representative of the Melbourne (
Underwriters’ Association, to load up with.
The contrast betw'een the two crafts was so 1
forcible that even unreflective Tom Mac. was i
struck by it, and hesitated, after hailing, as to t
whether it would not, as a mere matter of business,
be the better policy to give the commission direct c
to the Resolution instead of making it a subject of
competition. Before he could decide, however,
slim Jim Barton, owner and master of the Jessie
•lane, was clambering up the bank in response to
his call.
“ ’Morning, Mr. Mac. ! What’s up? Anything
fresh ?”
“ ’Morning, Jim. Yes; there’s a fresh freight
offering, but I am going to give you and the
Resolution the chance of it. It will all depend
upon who can get to Echuca first and back to
Mungadel !”
“Oil, racing !” Jim’s face, which had lit up
with the agent’s first words, lost its aspect of
animation. “ I’m not on. I can’t race, and I am
not going to run risks of that sort.”
“ Well,” said sympathetic Mac., “ ’tisn't exactly
a race, Jim. But, look here, wait till that lazy
lubber of a Linton comes. Damn ! You’d think
that chap was commodore of the Pacific squadron,
he puts on so much side ! Now, Linton —I say
Linton, Skipper Linton ! Hurry up, man !”
“ Aye, aye, Mr. M'Grundy—l’m coming ! ’ and,
slowly as became the master of the dashingest new
boat on the rivers—not (o mention his antecedents
as an officer of Money-Wigram’s line —he walked
from his state-room —they were “state-rooms ” on
the Resolution and “ cabooses ” on the Jessie Jane
—to the gangway. There he stopped.
“Breakfasted, Mr. M‘Grundy ?” he drawled.
<( “No,” growled the agent, losing patience.
“ But I’m just going to have a bite with Barton
here. I want to see you first—look sharp! ”
And while the dandy of the boating trade was
digesting the unceremonious speech in his slow
walk, Mac. turned to Barton and said: “Can’t
stand his damned nonsense, Jim, so I’ll take a bit
with you, if you don’t mind.”
_ “Very welcome, of course, Mr. Mac., but I can’t
give you ham and chicken and wine, you know.
We’ve only salmon and tea.” And Jim laughed.
“‘Better,’as Solomon says, ‘tinned fish and
billy tea where no side is than a French dinner with
show and bounce,’” said M‘Grundy, and then, as
Captain Linton drew near, he communicated his
news.
“ Look here, chaps, after you dropped down last
night Tom Lang, of Mungadel, came to me. He
says by the time you get to Echuca GO tons of
Ryland’s wire ought to be there. Now, it’s a
matter of the first importance that he should get
it here within a fortnight. It’s for his back
country, and he can get teams now for next to
nothing, and if he misses the chance, it’s all up
with his back country improvements for a year.”
“ Well,” broke in Linton, as Mao. stopped for
breath, “M'Culloch’s Lang’s agents—they can
run it down easily ! ”
“ That’s just it. They say they can’t. They’re
rather cocky and say wire-loading must wait their
boats’ convenience, and there’s a rush of higher-
priced freight, and they won’t take more wire than
makes a fair proportion of dead-Weight.”
“ That’s the case with everybody,” said Linton,
“unless,” he added, superciliously, “it suits
Barton here.”
“All right, Dick Linton, if you turn up your
nose at a good thing before you know what it is,
that’s your own look-out. If I hadn’t promised
Mr. Lang to put the proposal before you both, i
I'm blest if I wouldn’t give it Jim straight.”
Grundy was getting nettled.
“Well,” said Linton, now scenting something
beneath the surface, “ I don't see as there can be i
much in it for anybody when the big firm say no i
to it.”
“Ah, but the big firm haven’t had the chance i
of saying no to what I offer you two. Lang’s i
E temper's up and he’ll give special terms to got this
, wire here in the time.”
s “ What are they ?”
• “ Double rates for the wire, 30 tons Ballarat
chaff—must be Ballarat, if you can pick it up at
■ Echuca when you’re there without waiting— at six
ten—that ought to give you a couple of pounds a
ton profit—and ” He paused to give a rheto-
rical effect to his next sentence.
“ Well, don’t be all day, Mac, Time’s slipping
along !” said Linton.
“ All his lading down and up for this and the
next season !”
“By George, those are grand terms, Mr. Mac.,”
exclaimed Barton. “He must want that wire
badly.”
“He does. You see he’ll save about six
pounds per ton in back loading, and Heaven
knows how much by getting his back-blocks
fenced in before next year; besides, there’s the
dishing of M‘Culloch. That’s worth something
to a cove like Lang, who makes money himself
out of other people’s necessities.”
“But, Mr. Mac., what have we to do? Do we
get the loading between us?” questioned the Jessie
Jane’s skipper.
’ No, that’s not it. The first boat of you two
ready to take it from the cranes at Echuca wharf
has the whole contract. That’s the offer !”
The two skippers eyed each other. Barton, for
the life of him, could not prevent the blood rushing
into his cheeks, at the thought that, if he could
only pull off this job, what a tremendous difference
it would make to his fortunes. Linton’s personal
interest in the prospective achievement was not so
large as Barton’s, but it was still great. It would
only mean a big commission, but it would be
something to talk about. “How I did M'Culloch’s
out of the Mungadel contract ” would be a tale
that would tell well when conversing the next
time with the inspector of the Bank of Australia
Felix, who was finding the capital for the new
steamer-line to which the Resolution belonged.
Linton did not care much for monev, except as it
enabled him to play “the swell,” but he was as
vain as a peacock or a poet—Lord ! he’d be some-
body on the rivers if he could snatch the Munga-
del clip from the maw of the big firm. Who
knows but that, perhaps, the bank-inspector would
suggest that Linton be made commodore of the
new line, or perhaps build a boat—a regular clipper
it should be —for him ! And so, realising in a Hash
of thought all the offer involved, he jumped at it.
“I’m on, Mr. M‘Gnmdy ! I’ll do it if you’ll
■give me the order,” he said.
“No, Lang says I’m not to bind myself to either
boat. A fair field, he says, and no tricks, and it’s
as open to the Jessie as to the Resolution.”
“Oh ! but,” interjected Linton, “you’re out of
it from the start, Barton. Look at my haulage
power over .yours.”
“Look at your load to mine ! ” retorted Barton.
“You know what old Freeman said? It’s the
gossip of all the Riverine when he last surveyed
y°u. He said that if you put pressure on the
Jessie’s boiler—and you survived—he’d get your i
license cancelled. And without your license you
can’t insure, and without insurance much freight i
you’ll have !” Linton showed his dfsire for the i
contract by the bitterness of his sneer.
“ Well, ’ retorted Barton, “if the Jessie does
blow up, she’ll only kill six, and not sixty !”
A mighty guffaw from M'Grundy expressed his ;
appreciation, of Barton’s remark, and the angry
colour in Linton’s cheeks was other testimony to ]
its force. Barton had referred to a grim River i
tradition that Linton had had Ids mate’s certifi- i
cate cancelled by the Board of Trade because of -
i his running down in the English Channel a Greek
vessel with sixty lives on board. r I here were
at this time a score of “seagoing” officers
engaged in the river-trade—two or three (including
-Linton) m commands, several more as mates, most
as deck hands. One of two reasons—often the
two combined usually explained why these
manners had left the rolling wave for the river.
Ihink or disaster, if it were not drink and
disaster—one or the other, or one and the other.
And the suspicion of past failure being upon these
sons of the salt sea, they were not beloved or
admired by the Croweaters, and Cornstalks, and
Gumsuckers, who, with an ex-Mississipian or two,
bossed the river-craft 14 to 20 years ago.
Men to the rivers born were never tired of re-
peating how C ,of the Princess, whose certifi-
cates (in their uncancelled state) should have ob-
tained for him command of an Atlantic liner
hauled up at xiiidday at Pental Island. The river
was shallowing rapidly-five feet at Albury, 15 at
Jiichuca, and 10 at Swan Hill —and as every
moment was precious the mate objected. “ Wot
d’yer want stopping here for, shipper ?” “Oh ! ”
G > “I’m going to take an observation.
1 here’s no latitude or longitude marked on the
chart. “ II—1,” cried the mate, “ wot d’yer want
longtitood and latitood for? Ain’t yer got that
blawsted box yonder by the woodpile to steer by ?”
And the roar with which the anecdote was ever
welcomed, defined the precise degree of deprecia-
tion in which the “ocean-going” chaps were re-
garded by the river men proper.
Now, Barton was a Croweater, had been born on
the Lower Murray in the very year (’53) that
Cadeli had taken up the Lady'Augusta, and he
had grown up with the boat-trade. Consequently,
he was not predisposed to look upon Linton with
the friendliest eyes, and when he understood that
Linton was resolved to achieve the Mungadelcon-
tract, the getting of which would alter his
(Barton’s) whole career materially for the better,
he would have been more than human had he
refrained from meeting taunt with taunt. But,
to tell the truth, he felt for the moment that the
prize was as good as lost when he-saw how Linton
writhed under his retort, for then he knew that
neither fair nor foul means would be spared by the
Resolution’s skipper to get the job. To a man who
would not fight fair there were lots of ways open
of impeding a competitor for a cargo. A
pushed surreptitiously into a stevedore’s hand had
more than once led to the sinking of a barge as it
lay loaded beneath the cranes at Echuca wharf.
There was that Cumberoona epusode, when Harry
Clifton was ’Chuca manager for M‘Cullocb’“ -but
it must be a story for another time.
* * *
When M‘Grundy’s laugh had exhausted
itself, Linton, looking like a thunder-cloud, said ;
‘‘And what’s to be your share of the bunce,
M‘Grundy?”
The agent meditated for a second. Should it
be a p.n. at three months? —Mac.’s p.n. at three
days after Doomsday would have been negotiable
' or , there was that bay pony Tom Palmer, of
Caroon, had offered him for a song, cash ? Which
should it be? At last—
“ Usual agency charges, boys—no more; but
you know there’s a nice litt’e pony hack Tom
Palmer has—l wouldn’t refuse that from either of
you as an unofficial present, boys--”
“x\ll right, Mr. Mac., if I get the job,
ponys yours,” said Barton, and Linton also
acquiesced, but with sullenncss. It was well
enough for that fellow Barton to promise a bonus
when he ran no risk of being called upon to pay

it, but Linton thought he’d be dashed if he’d a
promised it if it had been left to himself, after the
way M’Grundy had laughed at him.
And so it was arranged. First under the
’Chuca cranes, ready to take in the wire, was to
have the whole Mungadel business for the just
opening season and the following year as well,
And when, after a hurried breakfast in the shabby
little cabin of the Jessie Jane, he hurriedly shook
hands with Barton, he fervently hoped the little
’un would pull the game off. “It’ll make you,
Jim, if you do,” he exclaimed.
-ry
Three days afterwards the boats were at
Swan Hill together. What the big boat gained
during the day she lost during the night.
Jim knew the river intimately, while the
skipper of the Resolution had to find his course
by the rude charts of the river men, and not being
backed by a life acquaintance with the char-
acteristics of the stream, he was often in a tangle.
When he found himself—as frequently happened
—gingerly steering through deep water where the
chart showed a sand-spit he had always a fear that
he might find a sand-spit calmly embracing a snag
where the paper marked 10 or 12 feet of water,
He was under the necessity accordingly of tying
up at nightfall. Jim, on the other hand, ran as
long as he could at night without breaking down
his engineer and fireman, while the man who
(assuming his certificates were all right) could have
taken a Cunarder from Mersey to Sandy Hook,
or brought out a P. and O. mail-boat from South-
hampton to Melbourne, was compelled to grin and
bear the sting as best he might when the Jessie,
which he had passed hours before, returned the
compliment after nightfall. The three sharp
whistles with which the Jessie would greet him
sounded saucily, and her exhaust, he could have
sworn, cheeked him. And, if those mechanical
insults were not sufflcient he would be compelled
to hear wordy taunts that incurably hurt his
vanity.
“ Skipper !” would sing out all the bargemen as
the Jessie Jane came abreast of the Resolution,
“blest if that ain’t Toff Linton’s boat! What’s
he waitin’ for?”
Jim would maintain a decent silence. Then the
bargeman would answer himself.
“ He’s hopin’surely as the moon’ll rise soon as
he can take the long’tood !”
Then the deck hands would join in ; “ No, that
’tain't it. He’s waitin’ for the dew to fall, so ho
can get some way on her.”
“You're wrong, all o’ y T ou,” would now inter-
ject the mate. “His crew’s struck—he war
pilotin’ the boat up the billabong backwater.”
“Ga-arn,” another rouseabout would sneer.
“He’s an appintment with Black Nell at the
Melool wood-pile, an’ he carn’t wax his moustache
while the boat’s a-steamin’, so he ties up.”
So the jokers of the river would dash their
humours against the sides of his cabin. Their
joking had the additional bitterness for Linton
that it was never answered by his own men. An
impressive characteristic of the river was that
the men of a boat were loyal to their skipper, as a
rule. Let the skipper be trying to make a point in
a trio, or to score off a rival—either by coarse
chaff qr sheer rapid steaming—and they would
help him with lung and limb, forfeit for him their
sleep and their meal-time, or even effect that most
precious of sacrifices, the abandonment of their
“ between-trip-sprees.” And when his men did
not champion a skipper through good report and
through ill report, and back him up in straight
river reaches and over the moral snags, generally
of the feminine order, that were always in readi-
ness on the shore to wreck any boat-chap, you
might safely depend that the skipper
“white.” Now, Linton’s crew never “jawed
back” when the Jessie Jane’s fellows hurled their
jibes at the Resolution’s chief, and Linton had
been long enough in the trade to take that
fact as an emphatic condemnation in advance
of anything he might do. And, therefore,
being full of human nature, and not being
a saint out of the missionary books, he grew
venomous and as tyrannical as he dared.
Certainly he did not dare greatly ; democracy was
too substantial a thing on the rivers 20 years ago
to be assailed with impunity by a little, tinselled
god. It is flabbier now : boat-hands deputationise
the deity of the pilot-house now where, in the
seventies, they would have seen whether the
Murray a’Bidgee water wouldn’t have taken the
lace-frilled nonsense out of him.
When the boats, then, were at Swan Hill it
was an even chance which would get through
first at ’Chuca, and so gain the first shot at the
cranes. The Resolution with her superior power
would, without doubt, beat the Jessie by four
hours if there was clear daylight running all the
way, and it would be a remarkable thing indeed
if,'with that advantage, she could not win the
Mungadel job. But the four hours were just the
difference that the Jessie could make up by ivght
travel in the 21 to 30-hour-run from Castle
Donnington (Swan Hill) to Echuca. Thus honors
were easy.
Linton and Barton met at the township, just as
the latter had despatched the wire most boat-
masters sent thence to Echuca.
Barton cheerily nodded a “Morning,’’ which
Linton surlily acknowledged.
“ It’ll be nose and nose work, Captain Linton,”
said the Jessie’s skipper.
“ Bosh. You’re out of it. I’m not going to
race, either ! ”
“Nor I—fair heel-and-toe running—that’s what
I’m going to make of it.”
“ I’d not bother if I were you. Save your fuel
and the risk of a blow up !”
“Not a bit of it, old man. See the * state ’ at
Albury last Saturday?” And he pointed as he
spoke to a bit of blue paper pasted at the Post
Office, which recorded the state of the Murray
“ above Summer level.”
“Yes, 20 feet—what of that?”
“ That means, old man, you’re not going to get
past Puggamilly till that water comes down. I
know you scraped getting over the Bitch and
Pups!”
“ How the devil do you know that. By George!
If any of my men have been blabbing !”
“Keep your temper—none of ’em’s been
blabbing. But, man, you needn’t tell me you
didn’t scrape. When you’re down eight feet, and
the water on the reef is eight feet also, you've got
to scrape to get over. And you can’t warp over
Puggamilly. I can if I need to !—but I won’t!”
“ Very well,” and Linton turned to go, “think
as you please. Puggamilly, or no Puggamilly,
I’m going to run Mungadel stores up and down
for two seasCns.”
“Perhaps you may, Linton, if I give you a
billet on the new barge I shall build as soon as I
finger the cash for the freight on the Mungadel
wire now waiting for meat Echuca wharf.”
Having fired this shot, Jim Barton prepared to
send his telegram to the ’Chuca agents that he
was just clearing from the “ Hill.” As he handed
it in, and threw down the shilling, the operator
who had heard part of his conversation with
Linton, asked him if he was racing the Resolution.
In a few hurried words, Jim related the conditions
of the trip. “Wish you luck, Jim, I’m sure,”
said the operator as he pulled down the glass slide
of the pigeonhole. “If you get the Mungadel
work, it’ll be the making of you.”
The words stuck persistently to Jim. lorn
M'Grundy had said the same thing. His mate
kept on saying it; now the E.T.O. man who knew
everybody’s concerns on the river and whom every-
body knew, repeated it. As for himself, since he
had started he had been trying to forget how
much depended on the trip up to now. Doing his
best to win, he had yet freed himself from think-
ing of the prize. But the blessed words stuck, and
do what lie could he could not forget them.
It would be the making of him indeed ! And
as the Resolution dropped into the stream, and
the Jessie Jane followed her after a five minutes’
interval, Jim, answering the former’s horn with
his shrill whistle, felt, as he turned the whistle
cock, he was defying fortune as well as Linton’s
boat. The one thing that saves commerce from
infecting with leprous taint all that which she
touches is her alliance with the genius of home and
the spirits of the household. Where this is absent
how contemptible is trade—how degrading the
whole business of barter and speculation !
Now, Jim thirsted for gold, not to gratify his
vanity or the mere lust of getting, but in order to
obtain the means of enriching his own individual
life with the good things that life in general offers
to man. He wanted gold to win and keep a wife,
and to rear “an independent shed.’’ He wanted
the means to enjoy honourable leisure, and for
books, travel, anything that could make him a
stronger and more fruitful man. He had ambi-
tions, had this mallee, river-bred man, that were
not bounded by the mallee-rim on the south side
of the Murray, or by the box and gums on the
north bank. Inch-by-inch he had carved a path
upward; inch-by-inch he would mount higher
still —if the Rates pleased. If a man’s sinews and
a man’s thoughts could turn the sparse oppor-
tunities which offered, into gold, they should be
his. And here, right here, was the biggest chance
yet within the possibility of his grasp. Rail now,
and probably he would run a mortgaged boat
until Providence pleased that the boiler should
blow up with himself on board. Get the Mung-
adel contract, and through the long vista of
coming years he saw himself moving on from
success to success and from triumph to triumph-
first in the commercial world, and then—the
ambition was so sacred as to be no more than half
uttered to his own heart —in the political world.
Jim, ordinarily clear-headed enough, was yet at
this time so simple as to believe that politics had
some use for clean-souled men.
HI.
Every steamer’s “exhaust” to all river people
had its own message. Hitherto the Jessie Jane’s
escaping steam had had but one reading : “ Kiss
Jessie —Jim ! Kiss Jessie —Jim ! Kiss Jessie —
Jim !” That was the mode of it. But this morn-
ing there was a distinctly audible change.
Whether it was the rotten old boiler had taken a
new lease of life, or that some part of the engine
had gone wrong, or, having previously gone wrong,
was now determining to go right, or something
still more recondite and unexplainable ; but cer-
tainly the sibilation was no longer to be heard.
Jim noticed it as soon as he got into a fair way.
He called down the speaking-tube to the engineer.
“ Something wrong with the exhaust, Charlie.”
Charlie came up to the main deck. “ Yes,
skipper, she’s changed her toon. Steampipe’s got
a bit choked, I expect. \Vhat does she say ?
‘ Breakin’somethin,’it sounds like.”
Make you, break you—Jim ! Make you,
break you—Jim! that’s what it is, skipper,”
called the mate. “Well, that’s dashed funny,
isn t it r .
Very !’ replied J im, who was leaningover the
half-door of the pilot-house. And his heart
throbbed painfully as the alternative to being
made was voiced by the steam. Of course,
that was nonsense. If he did not get the Munga-
del contract, he would be no worse off than before,
And if he did well, bed be made. Mortgage
paid off ami open ciedit for a round thousand over-
draft at the bank. Then he would build a new
barge for next season and with the prestige of one
of the lug frontage station contracts behind him,
>o would no be dependant on agents’ mercies,
but won d tack e l alt-a_do7eback-blocks
stations himself. 1 nil off Mungadel, and he would
not change P aces with M Cullochs ! Ihe next
season would then see him worth three thousand
and all squarely earned, clean money. How many
of the river men would be able to say the same ?
By Heavens, he would have the contract And,
to cheer him, came another tune on the exhaust :
“Make you make you-Jmi.
That was it then ! Even the crazy old boat and
toiler were p ent u p on winning. Listen again 1
Make raa ke yoa —Jim.” Nothing more

not compelled to warp he could (by putting on
another 10 pounds of steam) overtake the Jessie
Jane, and beat her through the Echuca port.
And as he put out his big lamps he cursed the
regulations which compelled him to aid in the
illumination of the reach. The more light in so
stiff a part of the river the better for his rival,
But the broad flashes, Jim noticed, were not
the steady fan-shaped beams of the Resolution’s
lamps. They were intermittent, now wavered by
the breeze, now blown by it into vivid, lightning-
like zig-zags that shivered and darted into the

And a figure was now seen in the blaze, and was
now hidden by it.
“Yes,” said Jim, “that’s a signal or else a
dodge. P’r’aps the Resolution has sent a man
there to trick us. Anyhow, if it’s a passenger,
we’re not going to take him.”
Eor, of course, if it were a passenger—and
would-be passengers emerged from the most uu-
likely places on the rivers—it must be a male.
None but a crank would have supposed a woman
would be there.
•
it
.
Yet, when the Jessie came abreast of the lower
point of Puggamilly- she took the inner channel
because of the lighter draught—Skipper Barton
saw that it was a woman who was standing by the
signal fire—a woman holding something in her
arms even when she stooped to throw another
branch of sapling on the flame.
“Jupiter,”, cried the mate, “It’s a petticoat.”
Jim, prescient of trouble, was silent. And the
infernal exhaust was now humming another and
more sardonic tune: “Break you, break you,
Jim.” Nothing about “ making ” him now,; only
“breaking.”
“Slow her down ! ’ Jim ordered at last. And
the craft trembled reluctantly to half-speed.
“Oh!” the woman cried, ” Oh, stop—for God’s
sake, stop ! ” And the Jessie, with much inward
and outward cursing from her officers, was eased
down about 20 yards from the island-bank.
“ What is it, ma’am!” Jim spoke with marvel-
lous distinctness, considering his throat was full of
maledictions. “Going to Echuca ? ”
“No; Swan Hill !”-she stopped, choking.
dim gave a sigh of relief before he spoke again.

“ Impossible, ma’am. I’ll take von to Echuca
-there are better doctors there.”
“ But Swan Hill’s nearer-three hours, and-

Jim and the mate looked at one another. Then
the mate uttered an enquiry as to whether she
ad asked the other boat.
“Yes,” she wailed, “ but they would not listen.
Said you were cornin’. Oh, for God’s sake, take
me ! I have a pound-it is all I have. I’ll give
y °A pound! Jim’s throat blistered. And fifteen
hundred pounds clear profit hanging on that trip !
A pound ! The joke of it ! And so he said
“No,” and in the same breath sent the telegraph-
call to the engine-room—“ Full speed ahead !”
When the woman saw the Jessie’s stem curving
towards midstream, she shrieked with a horrible
acuteness of sound. Over the exhaust was
plainly audible—“ Oh, are you men ? Fiends you
are ! May God blight your homes—your wives—
your children ! May you have your little ones
dyin’ for the sake of a doctor that someone else
could reach, but wouldn’t-oh, devils !”
Now, in Jim’s dream that day of what might be
if he pulled off the Mnngadel job was included a
home and a wife and babies clustering round him.
There was no wife as yet—not even a sweetheart,
But he had always thought that the glory of his
manhood would never be achieved till he held
wife and child in the sway of his strong arm.
And the woman’s unreasoning cry pierced him
with a superstitious fear.
He signalled the engineer to slow down.
“But,” he asked the mate, “is there a man aboard
who’d take that woman in a dingy to Swan Hill?”
“ A forty-mile pull ! You’re drunk, boss.”
“No, Bill—not drunk ! Only mad ! I’m going
to take that woman to the Hill ! Damn her !
and damn Tom Lang, of Mnngadel!”
-y- m
■
Less for the sake of that poor selector’s
wife than for his own dream wife and child he
tied up his barge and turned the steamer’s head
towards the Hill township.
The woman’s only intelligence lay in her
mother-love. She was one of those half-sonled
creatures that drift through life in the Australian
bush, going through most of the acts of the great
drama of existence in a tadpolish fashion, but who
suddenly rise in the crisis of an episode to some-
tiling like the stature of a civilized woman,
When her child was seized with convulsions she
had tramped across the scrub and waded lagoons
ten miles from her husband’s selection—he was
away shearing—to the riverbank, with a dim hope
to obtain a doctor somehow. It was really Linton
who had put the idea into her head to ask Barton
to take her to Swan Hill. With the ignorant
woman’s inability to look at more than one thing
at a time she became convinced that in going to
the Hill lay hope for her child ; and, in the tragic
moment, when she made her last and successful
appeal to Jim, she had struck the one chord of his
nature to which his will would respond,

At least, all that fell within human ken.
“ All-judging Jove ” may, however, have viewed

The Jessie Jane was still breathing her fatal
exhaust “ Break you, break you—Jim !” when she
reached Puggamilly again. By that time twenty-
four hours’ steaming had done its work on the
engineer and fireman. She was hauling in the
tow-rope of the barge, and the latter craft was
just beginning to cut the stream, when—
The Jessie Jane—and her skipper, and mate,
and engineer, and one deck hand—and the hopes
of the Mungalel contract—and the mortgage went
skywards (and literally on to Puggamilly Island)
at the instance of the Jessie Jane’s boiler. The
pressure-gauge had registered more and more as
the eyelids of engineer Charlie and his fireman
had become heavier, and ten minutes after the
eyelids had closed entirely the index-hand of the
gauge reached what old Freeman called “explo-
sion point.” Charlie had been roused by the
skipper hailing the barge when the Jessie reached
Puggamilly, but, sti 1 drowsy, had not noticed the
gauge, till the sudden hiss of the enfranchised
steam merged and thundered into a roar like the
trumpet of doom. And that was the ending of
everything for the Jessie Jane and her crew.
When the Echuca Steam Navigation Board
investigated the circumstance, sufficient came out
in the evidence of his own men to take some of
the gilding from the glory of Skipper Lintons
achievement. And he received a round robin
from the boat skippers one day which suggested
that he should return to the ocean-going trade,
and run down some more Greeks. They wanted
“ white ” men on the river—so the note said. Tom
M‘Grundy didn’t get his pony—then.

A Remarkable Letter.
(To the Editor of the New York Tribune.)
Sib, —As my name and face have appeared in
your paper and the public prints lately, and
as many of my professional brethren are wonder-
ing at it, I feel it only just that I should make an
explanation. The statement published over my
name was made ten years ago, after long and
mature investigation, and I have never changed
my mind as to the facts then stated. At that
time I said, as a physician, that I believed
Warner’s Safe Cure was the best of all known
preparations for the troubles it was advertised to
cure, and I say so still. I know it is considered
the proper thing for the medical profession to
decry proprietary and other advertised articles;
but why should they do so? As the late Dr. J.
G. Holland, writing over his own name in
Scribner's Monthly, said
“It is a fact that many of the best proprietary
medicines of the day are more successful than
many physicians, and most of them were first
discovered or used in actual medical practice;
when, however, any person knowing their virtue
and foreseeing their popularity secures and
advertises them, in the opinion of the bigoted all
virtue went out of them.”
Dr. Holland was an educated physician, an
unprejudiced observer, and he spoke from a broad
and unusual experience. Proprietary medicines
should not be decried. The evidences of their
value are overwhelming. I have seen patients
recover from gravel, inflammation of the bladder,
and Bright’s Disease after using Warner’s Safe
Cure, even when all other treatment has failed.
I make this frank and outspoken statement in
the interests of humanity, and because I know it
to be true. I trust for the same reason you will
give it to the public.
Respectfully, R.'A Gunn.
124 West 47-street, New York, March 1.
Mr. Gunn is Dean of the United States Collego >
New York, and Editor of the Medical Tribune.

The Modern Shepherd.
(FOR THE BULLTIN.)
(Respectfully dedicated to the superior clergy throughout
Australia, in recognition of the eminent services they
are at present rendering the new Democracy.)
The Parson rose from his downy bed at the break
of the Sabbath Day,
lie dressed himself and decked himself in the
chaste aestiietio way—
Lavender-scented was his garb, rings graced his
lily hand ;
In truth he was a goodly sight—the parson of the
land.
The parson drove to the temple fair where the
Lord of Hosts abode,
And his carriage flew, at a rattling pace, on the
wide and dusty road
(Tis true the Master went afoot, in the days of
long ago,
Nor-pur sc nor scrip his followers bore; but the
v >tbnes have altered so).
- i*-
The* parson stood in the pulpit then and gazed
upon his flock —
The big land-sharks and the business men (the
Church re* ts on a rock !)
The big fat paunch, and the big gold guard, anti
the broadcloth shone so sleek —
’Twas a sight divine —the stately shrine of the
Lowly and the Meek.
The parson waved bis lily hand and raised his
mellow voice,
“The Lord hath been exceeding good. ILjoice !
my friends, rejoice !
The oil of gladness hath He poured in fullness on
each head,
His little lambs, in pastures green, by running
waters, led.
“ Let the men of substance raise the hymn, the
upper orders sing ;
Ye bankers! make the holy aisles of the sanctuary
ring;
Ye landlords ! join the tuneful throng and laud
Him loud who said
The landlord had not left Him where to lay His
gracious head.
“Lift up the psalm, ye usurers! the New Day
doth begin ;
Christ turned the money-changers out —but now
wo take them in ;
The creed was somewhat narrow which the
Saviour preached of old,
The simple Galilean—but we’re making wide the
fold.
“Lift up your songs, ye sons of pelf—ye burly men
of beer !
And sing, in soft, respectful tones, ye poor folks in
llv rear !
Lo ! There be many mansions in the Father’s
house on high
E'en tiie poor man, if respectful, hath a lodging
in the sky.
“Lord ! Strike the Sons of Belial low who dare to
rise and kick ;
Lord ! finite the Agitator home and touch him
to the quick;
Lord ! Guard full well the powers that be and
keep our pastures green,
Our people meek and humble, as they hitherto
have been.
“Oh, Lord ! The Discontented—they be many in
the land,
They love not us, the sanctified, Thine own
anointed band ;
Make bare Thy strong right arm and help Thy
chosen in this town —
Christ ‘ stirred the people up’ of old—but do Thou
keep them down.
“ Lord ! Send thy choicest blessing down on all
who have a stake
In this Thy pleasant vineyard. Let the scoffer
fear and quake.
Lord ! Prosper all our little specs., let corner
’lotments rise ;
So shall we win our way at last to ‘corners’ in the
skies.”
At last the shepherd ended. Then out the
bankers hied;
The land-sharks and the usurers they followed
side by side ;
The fat man with the stake came next, and then
the man of beer,
And lastly came the poor man, so respectful, in the
rear.
Then the parson left the holy place and gat him
home with speed,
And he sate him down to a royal feast (the wine
was choice, indeed);
And the servants bowed to every beck of the
shepherd’s lily hand —
In truth, he was a goodly sight, the parson of the
land !
Mount Morgan.
P. LUFTIG.

Lydia Foote, the news of whose deatli was received by
the last English mail, stood, in her good time, some
fifteen or twenty years ago, in the very front rank of
emotional actresses, but she was seldom afforded an
opportunity of showing what she could do with a great
part. Old London playgoers will remember her Esther
Eocles, in ‘‘Caste,” especially in the final scene, where
she surprised her father in the act of attempting to steal
her child’s coral. George Honey', the original Eccles, hut
in many respects the inferior of Anson in the part, seemed
to curl up under the withering contempt of her glance,
and on the first night of the performance of the comedy
at the Prince of Wales’ the house fairly rose at the
actress. Lydia Foote, however, never had such another
chance, and though she always acted conscientiously and
at times showed an exquisite and delicate sense of pathos,
she had the misfortune to be cast principally in second-
rate melodramas, which have only an ephemeral success.
She was a niece of the veteran Mrs. Keeley.

ment.

Dear Bulletin, — Apropos a Poverty Point par.
drawing attention to the likeness of Anson to Coqnelin, it
may he worth mentioning that the English comedian,
while in Australia, was upon one occasion mistaken for
the greatest actor on the French stage. It happened
during a visit of the “divine George ” to the French flag-
ship, and as soon as the crew caught sight of him they
called out with one voice, “Ah! Cest Coqnelin!”
Nothing could persuade them, especially when Anson
addressed them in their native tongue, that their Parisian
favourite, eliminating time and space, had not made his
first appearance in Australia on hoard a man-of-war, and
they are probably of the same opinion still. To those
who have seen both actors, the resemblance is simply
marvellous, while their art-methods are strikingly
similar. There is nobody on the English stage at the
present time at all comparable to Anson in his own line,
and if he gets the show he will certainly “ knock them
when he appears there.— Egomet.
